<answer>



The British system of sentencing is far different from the American. Here, it is very unlikely that anyone serves a full life sentence, for example. There are sentencing guidelines which judges take into account - I don't think such exist in the USA. On the other hand, we are far more strict about guns. The American system seems to be (looking at it as an outsider) far more designed as a punitive system only, ours has ideals of being not only that but reformatory also. Whether it works is a different matter. Which is better is a matter for constant debate. It does seem to me, however, that there is more crime in the USA than here, by and large.



Source(s):



Retired criminal lawyer.



</answer>

<question>



Mens rea with regard to careless driving?



I'm not sure if I have understood this concept so could someone let me know if this sounds right?
I am doing a report for my studies on a case I watched in court where the defendant was charged with driving without due care and attention.  I am writing about the moral side of the offence (he dropped a cigarette on his leg and was distracted) and I don't think he did anything morally wrong.  I was going to say that if he was intentionally driving dangerously he would, under mens rea (did I use this correctly?) be morally guilty, but as he was charged with careless driving he is not morally guilty.  Is this right or way off?  Can anyone help please?  Thank you!



</question>

Also, some crimes are malum en se, or bad in itself. (murder) Others are malum en prohibitum, bad because the legislature says so. (not wearing a seatbelt)

<answer from>



on thin ice
Member since: 22 September 2006
Total points: 4583 (Level 4)



</answer from>



<answer>



The expressions you need to try and understand are:



'mens rea' which is taken to mean the guilty state of mind,



and 'actus reus' taken to mean the actual guilty actions.



Much simplified, it is sometimes said that 'they can't lock you up for what you are thinking, only when you put your thoughts into action' .. a way of explaining that for the majority of offences there has to be those two elements coming to-gether. So you may well see that an Act of Parliament may say something like: ( eg for burglary it is an offence ) ' if a person enters a building as a trespasser with the intention of stealing anything inside ' the two element are 1) entry as a trespasser ( the actus reus ) and 2) with the intention ( the mens rea )of stealing . Mere entry as a trespasser to sleep is not burglary it lacks a mens rea to steal , being there legally and then stealing is not burglary but would be simple theft.



Sometimes some offences are termed 'absolute offences' , or 'offences of strict liability', usually situations where the public interest, safety and protection takes priority over the thoughts that may or more importantly were absent from the offenders mind. Since this concept of absolute liability is serious the legislation will need to make it clear that offences are to be so treated and unless this is clear the courts will not find that strict liability exists. For the legally minded look at the case of Sweet v Parsley [1970] AC132 .. concerning a property owners liability when occupants used the house for smoking drugs.



Examples of strict liability would be driving with excess alcohol .. the proof of being over the limit enough to justify a conviction. Public health and food hygience offences often fall into the category of absolute offences.



Source(s):



case refs. as given which I remember from law school.



</answer>



<answer from>



Law student
Member since: 02 June 2008
Total points: 798 (Level 2)



</answer from>



<answer>



There are two types of laws and legal violations;
Malum Prohibitum  (bad because it is prohibited)
Malum In se



(action is ban by its nature)



To the extent that violating the law is bad, all Malum Prohibitum crimes are morally wrong.  We also know however that there may be unjust laws and the like.  Thus crimes do not always coincide with moral wrongs.



As to your intent question, yes you got the mens rea bit correct.  Mens rea is a term generally indicating the scienter element of a crime, or the criminal intent level required to convict.  The model penal code generally gives 5 levels of mens rea,



Purposeful
Knowledge
Reckless
Negligent
Absolute (strict) liability



At least in the eyes of the law the wrong action was smoking while driving.  The smoking was wrong exactly because he could get distracted by the fumes, or burned, and thus drive in an unsafe fashion.  Naturally we know that many people do not consider smoking wrong, or even naturally reacting to the burn as wrong.  The moral issue, if any, arises from the chance that something bad could happen while smoking and driving.



Certainly there is only a small chance of something happening.  Many people drive doing things that are less safe everyday.  However what the government is essentially alleging is that society has decided, through the legislature and driving laws, that distracted driving is not acceptable.  Following from that, it doesn't matter how minor the distraction was, or what it was, only that he allowed himself to drive while distracted.



</answer>



<answer from>



waldo_mc...
Member since: 16 December 2007
Total points: 8083 (Level 5)



</answer from>



<answer>



'Mens rea' and criminal 'intent' are not always the same thing.Nor is 'mens rea' the same as moral guilt.



Each criminal offence has its own 'mens rea' and 'actus reus'.'Actus reus' is the result prohibited by law which your actions have caused.To be guilty of the offence you must also have had the required  'mens rea' at the time.'Mens rea'  generally but not always means 'intent' but more accurately is the state of mind the accused needs to be in at the time if the offence is to be proved.



Generally there needs to be a deliberate and voluntary act designed to bring about a particular state of affairs e.g inflicting serious harm intending to kill is murder.Equally you can be guilty of manslaughter for instance if your reckless or grossly negligent act results in someone's death even though you had no intention of causing death.



So 'mens rea' is about state of mind not morality as such.It is clear that the basic common law offences of murder robbery burglary etc. correspond directly with established notions of moral guilt.But many modern offences (particularly those relating to road use) are breaches of organisational regulations or standards which do not obviously correspond to breaches of established morality.So 'careless driving' is committed is someone's standard of driving falls below what is expected of a reasonably competent driver.'Mens rea' as such is not an issue. Hope this helps clarify it.



</answer>

<question>



Which state would I have to file a lawsuit in?



I bought an AKC certified "Boxer" from a breeder in PA when she was 8 weeks old.  As she is getting older she looks NOTHING like a Boxer so I questioned the AKC and they are now investagating the Breeder since they also believe she is not a purebreed Boxer.  If she is proven to NOT be a full breed Boxer I want to sue since I bought her thinking she was a Boxer not a mixed breed.  I live in NY but the breeder is in PA.  Which state would I have to sue in and what is the most I could sue for?  I love the dog, but have young children and bought this dog (at 8 weeks you really couldn't tell what she was she was so small) under the assumption that since she was AKC registered that I was buying a Boxer.  I have no idea what she is mixed with Pit Bull, Mastiff - who knows?  Now I have committed myself to this dog for the next I don't know how many years and I wanted a Boxer.  I would feel terrible to give her away since she is now 9 months old and my daughter is very attached to her, but I feel scammed.  Would I sue in PA since that's where she was purchased or in the state I live in NY?



</question>



</answer from>



<answer>



File in Pennsylvania, where the contract was made.



You could sue for the purchase price; and any incidental damages, like vet bills; and possibly punitive damages, in a fraud case.  But, the law considers a dog property, so if you win, you'll have to give the dog back.



You could, however, for the sake of you daughter, sue for the difference between the price you paid for the dog, and the price to adopt a mutt from the local shelter, and keep the dog.  And it doesn't hurt to ask for a few hundred dollars in punitive damages.



</answer>

<question>



Instead of paying, i get a longer lunch. How many times must my employer go wrong before I begin to justify?



Non-Exempt Employees
Employees classified as Non-Exempt will be paid 1-½ times their regular hourly rate of pay for all hours worked over 40 in a workweek. A workweek is the 7-day payroll week that begins on Sunday of each week and ends on Saturday. Hours worked include all time that a Non-Exempt employee performs duties that benefit the Bank and/or its customers or employees.  Such time specifically includes meal periods of less than 30 minutes and/or rest breaks of 15 minutes in length. Hours worked, for purposes of calculating overtime, specifically exclude any hours paid for time not worked such as vacation, absence, compensatory time, jury duty, bereavement, or holidays.



Under no circumstances are Non-Exempt employees to be given or offered compensatory time-off in lieu of payment for any time worked whether overtime hours or not.  Nor may a Non-Exempt employee be requested or allowed to work off-the-clock, i.e., work any hours that are not accurately reported on the employee’s Weekly Time card.

Taking pictures of the interior of an apartment before your first move in is the best protection.  If there are no pictures of the torn carpet, it is all over.

<answer>

The best time to do this is at about nine o'clock in the morning, before things get too busy.

<question>



Does the Speaker of the House have the authority to declare martial law on the US?



There has been debate whether the martial law declared by Palosi was actual military martial law or a tactic used to move a bill through without giving members ample to time to read and understand it before they vote on it. Who has the authority to declare military martial law on the US?



</question>



<answer from>



rickinno...
Member since: 13 May 2007
Total points: 39481 (Level 7)



Contributing In:



Law & Ethics



</answer from>



<answer>



<evaluation>Best Answer - Chosen by Asker</evaluation>



No, she doesn't, and no, she didn't.



The term "martial law" as she used it is a slang term for an emergency legislative session where the normal time frames for debating bills are waived. Such sessions have occurred many times before, and the members of the house all knew exactly what she was talking about.



I'm no fan of Pelosi - who I believe is the quintessential "Limousine Liberal" - a billionaire that claims to be a Socialist supporter of "the little people" (though not, of course, of the illegal alien grape pickers she was caught paying less than 1/2 minimum wage to!) - but this is a storm in a tea cup.



A true declaration of martial law is the responsibility of the executive, not legislative, branch.



Martial law can be declared by a Governor, within his State only, or by the President.



Richard



</answer>



<asker's comment>



This is also what I thought, thanks to everyone for the answers.



</asker's comment>



<answer from>



Charles G
Member since: 21 July 2008
Total points: 6609 (Level 5)



Contributing In:



Law & Ethics



United States



</answer from>



<answer>



Martial law is when military authority is imposed rather thant he usual police force. It has happened several times in American history. Lincoln declared it. It was declared in Hawaii in 1941. Something like it was declared in New Orleans after hurricane Katrina.



The executive branch has that authority, i.e., the President, governors of states, etc. It cannot be declared if the courts are operating. Usually, it is only declared in times of emergency or war.



I believe, if Ms. Palosi declared "martial law," she was using it as a figure of speech. Otherwise, I cannot see her handing such power to President Bush.



</answer>

<question from>



pavlin g
Member since: 09 April 2007
Total points: 1090 (Level 3)



</question from>



<question>



Are some laws just meant to be read but not executed?



For example, it is illegal to smoke if you are younger than 18 years of age, but at my school, minors smoke in front of the teacher. It is illegal to bring iPods to school (some schools) but students listen to music in class and the teacher says, "Nice iPod. Can hear a couple of songs?" I got many more examples.



</question>



<additional details>



Yeah, they do smoke right in fron of the cops, really!



</additional details>



<answer from>



Gerry Mander
Member since: 05 June 2008
Total points: 951 (Level 2)



</answer from>



<answer>



<evaluation>Best Answer - Chosen by Asker</evaluation>



There is a difference between Law and Policy as there is between Felony and Misdemeanor.  Policy, such as when a bank says, "...you may write 10 checks for free but after that all checks will cost 10 cents each..."  In the case of some depositors (businesses) the bank may "disregard" the 10 cent fee.



A law, on the other hand, may too be disregarded.  Underage smoking is a good example.  Since a lot of people do it, the enforcement effort alone, e.g., writing tickets, arresting people, throwing them in jail, costs more than what society would realize from it.  Writing a ticket means the state must pay someone to write the ticket, someone to print the ticket book, the purchase of a pen, the training of the person to write the ticket, the health care benefits and salary of that person, the judge who would hear the ticket case, the lawyer for both the prosecution and the defendant, the court time of the judge's clerk, his secretary, and on and on.  The total fine realized is often $50.00 while the total cost is over $1,000.



Often, such "laws" or "policies" are simply ignored.  Why bother?



Other crimes, like rape, burglary, etc. are prosecuted even though the actual dollar cost is usually much greater than the dollar compensation when the perpetrator is convicted.  There is compensation to society, however, and it is called "social capital."  Social Capital (Prof. James Samuel Coleman, American Journal of Sociology, June 1985, Supplement) is anything that facilitates individual or collective action, generated by networks of relationships, reciprocity, trust, and social norms.



Source(s):



I studied with him.



</answer>

<question from>



D. W.
Member since: 23 June 2008
Total points: 267 (Level 2)



</question from>



<question>



What should be done, who can he contract?



He buy everything his son needs; diapers, milk, clothes, etc... Mother tells the father can’t see his son unless he does everything she says. Take parenting classes, he has, take anger management classes he has (never needed them) but still he is not allow to see his son.  He works and she says “stop working and babysit your son or you won’t be able to see him anymore”. She told him that she has a restraining order against him(witch it’s a lied), so he doesn’t  go to the house, but still he has to take diapers, milk, clothes, etc... “so how am I suppose to do this if theirs a restraining order against me” he tells her? He doesn’t do what she says as soon as she says it than she tells him, “ You don’t love your son, I’m taking him to a different state”. If he calls to see how his son is or he doesn’t call she gets angry. He now has someone else call her to see how his son is. He called her to say that he wanted to see his son for his birthday, she said yes but only if you pay for half of the party. He had bought some clothes for his son because he outgrew clothes he had and with winter around the corner he needed warm clothing. But he was told to just leave the gift on the door step and to ring the bell. He loves his son, not a day goes by without him thinking about him. What should he do?



</question>



</answer from>



<answer>



File a petition to establish paternity.  You do that with the child support division of the county prosecutor's office.  There's usually a walk-in office at the local department of child/family/human services.



The court will set orders for support and visitation.

<question from>



Back of the net...!
Member since: 07 March 2008
Total points: 2181 (Level 3)



</question from>



<question>



If someone is ordered to pay money for driving without due care and attention?



What is it likely to be for?  As in, the amount is about £350 so this would seem too small for compensation.  Is it likely to just be a general 'fine' or what would be a good guess?  Court charges?  Administration?  Any ideas welcomed!



</question>



<answer from>



Azuriel8...
Member since: 01 October 2008
Total points: 127 (Level 1)



</answer from>



<answer>



Before I go into my response, I should point out that I have never practised law in the UK (although I have in Australia and a number of asian countries); however, many similarities can be drawn between these legal systems.



In answering your question.  "Driving without due care and attention" seems to imply that either another party other than the driver was harmed directly, or that the driver breached a duty of care he/she owed to another party, but without causing any actual harm.



In the first instance, that is, another party suffered actual damages, the money would go towards compensation for the most part, "costs" if the issue went to court, which includes administration fees, court charges, and usually the victim's legal fees.  While compensation for medical expenses or loss of income would normally be expected to be higher than the amount you've stated, you should also consider the possibility of property damage.  for instance, the driver may have swiped a wheelie bin, or knocked over someone's letterbox, and the cost of compensation would only be for replacement.



In the 2nd instance, that is, no actual damages were incurred, most of the cost would be a fine.  It is generally a tenet of the law that all drivers who hold a valid driving license acknowledge that they owe other users of public roads a duty of care, and agree to uphold this duty.  When this duty has been breached, the police will issue a fine.



What i've highlighted here are just the general points; UK law may have additional points which i've not yet covered, perhaps someone else with more knowledge on that area could add a few comments?



Cheers,
Ãz.



Source(s):



years of experience practicing law.



</answer>



<answer from>



JZD
Member since: 10 May 2006
Total points: 10121 (Level 6)



Contributing In:



Law & Ethics



</answer from>



<answer>



Compensation would only be ordered where there was a victim.



It may be the fine (it's a bit small) but I suspect it's a contribution towards the prosecution costs.



Source(s):



I'm a lawyer



</answer>



<answer from>



old.holl...
Member since: 12 September 2008
Total points: 1381 (Level 3)



Contributing In:



Law & Ethics



</answer from>



<answer>



There are two elements to the money that someone is ordered to pay.  The first part is the fine - the punishment for committing a crime and getting caught.  The second element is costs - this is to cover part of the costs of bringing the prosecution.



Additionally, there may be a third element in some instances.  If someone suffers in consequence of the offence, such as the driver going into another vehicle, the court may also award compensation to the victim.



</answer>

Azuriel8...

Total points: 127 (Level 1)



</answer from>



<answer>



First off, sorry about how you must be feeling at the moment.



I'll try to answer your question from a couple of angles:
1. Opening someone else's mail is usually a felony, but may not be, depending on which area of jurisdiction you live in.
2. They may have just opened it without checking who the envelope was addressed to, and may not have looked at the contents after realising it wasn't for them.
3. Yes, your passport number can be changed, go contact the department that issued your passport.
4. If you're worried about the bank statements, depending on which bank, you might be able to change the account number without being required to close the current account and opening another one.  Talk to the bank, explain you situation and concerns.  Generally they'll be sympathetic, since problems with your money are their problems too.
5. If, as a worst case scenario, something bad does happen, the employee who made the mistake is liable for all damages incurred by you, PLUS further compensation for anxiety and distress ... this last option depends on how far you want to take things, though ...



Hope some of that helped.



Cheers,
Ãz.



Source(s):



working in the field.



</answer>

<question>



California has a law that says a person has to fill a prescription within 5 weeks or face fines and/or jail?



Does this just mean that if a person fills a prescription more than 5 weeks after it was written then they can get in trouble? or does it mean that if a doctor writes a prescription that a person has to fill it no matter what? What if the prescription accidentally got destroyed?



</question>



<answer from>



mikey
Member since: 22 May 2008
Total points: 8580 (Level 5)



</answer from>



<answer>



<evaluation>Best Answer - Chosen by Asker</evaluation>



Interesting, actually that is not correct, and most of the respondents are incorrect also.  Although I do not practice any longer in California, I did for years, and I still retain my license there.  Expiration of Rx's are only a concern for certain types of medication, specifically opiates, certain other analgesics, and tranquilizers.  Most medications written for chronic diseases, such as diabetes, glaucoma or MS, have an expiration date on the Rx, placed by the physicians office, in order to encourage the patient to return for their regular evaluations.  There are no fines, or jail time, unless you decide to forge or steal Rx's from another patient...relax, the pill police are not on their way.



Source(s):



old doc



</answer>



<asker's comment>



i guess you would know



</asker's comment>



<answer from>



It's That Guy
Member since: 07 December 2007
Total points: 37148 (Level 7)



Contributing In:



Elections



</answer from>



<answer>



I think you have it wrong. When a doctor writes a prescription, it expires after 5 weeks, and a pharmacy will no longer fill it.  Actually, what really happens is the pharmacy calls the doctor and he tells them it's okay to fill it or not.  Either way, you won't get in trouble unless you do something fraudulent, like forging a prescription or trying to change the date.  These days, pharmacies nearly always call your doctor anyway when they fill a prescription.



You certainly don't have to fill a prescription a doctor gives you.  You have the choice of accepting or refusing any medical treatment (so long as you're an adult and mentally competent).  I have often had doctors write me prescriptions for pain killers after minor surgery, and then decided I didn't need them so I didn't bother with them.  I never had police show up and my door and force the pills down my throat.  8^)



If a prescription expires, you can call your doctor and he will write you a new one (if the drug still applies).  He will even fax it to the pharmacy.  They do stuff like this all the time.



</answer>



<answer from>



gomanyes
Member since: 24 June 2008
Total points: 7046 (Level 5)



</answer from>



<answer>



There is no law forcing you to fill a prescription.  If you attempt to fill it more than 5 weeks after it was written, it will be declined.



</answer>



<answer from>



Gypsy Girl
Member since: 13 August 2006

<question from>



nikki413...
Member since: 04 February 2008
Total points: 130 (Level 1)



</question from>



<question>



Are Human Rights issues always handled by NGOs? ?



Is this true? Wouldn't more be accomplished if there were a branch of government that dealt with this?



</question>



<answer from>



Kizzle
Member since: 14 September 2008
Total points: 987 (Level 2)



</answer from>



<answer>



There is, but it's usually the NGOs that make them do their job.



</answer>



<answer from>



Athena Kolbe
Member since: 27 December 2006
Total points: 192 (Level 1)



</answer from>



<answer>



No, not always. Nongovernmental organizations are simply non-profit, religious, charitable or social service agencies (what we might call a 501c3 in the United States). Some deal with human rights issues and some do not. Because NGOs are generally concerned with social issues such as labor, women's' rights, the environment, children, etc, they often intersect with human rights issues - but NGO's as a rule don't always deal explicitly with addressing human rights.



In terms of a branch of government dealing with human rights, some countries do have this. For instance, in the District of Columbia, the local municipal government has an office of human rights the deals with rights violations at the policy and implementation levels. See - http://ohr.dc.gov/ohr/frames.asp?doc=/oh...  In the US, at the national level we have the Office for Civil Rights, which is part of the Dept of Education. This office deals with discrimination and violation of the human rights of students, particularly those from vulnerable populations, such as students with disabilities. See - http://www.ed.gov/about/offices/list/ocr... .



Other countries have similar government offices as well, such as Australia (see http://www.hrc.act.gov.au/) and the UK (see for example http://collections.europarchive.org/tna/...



Source(s):



http://www.ed.gov/about/offices/list/ocr...
http://ohr.dc.gov/ohr/frames.asp?doc=/oh...
http://www.hrc.act.gov.au/
http://collections.europarchive.org/tna/...



</answer>

My mother once bought a box of antique picture frames from an auction, like super-duper cheap. As she got to unpacking them and deciding where to put them, etc. she opened one and found a $20 bill. She got excited, so she opened another, and found another $20 bill. She kept opening them, and found cash in every frame, which added up to $200. We were all excited and kept the money.



Now, ethically, what would you have done? Kept the money? Or would you have returned it to the auction site and told them you found the money and that whoever had the picture frames should keep it, since it was their money they put there for safe-keeping, or whatever reason they put it there. After all, it was rightfully their money and wouldn't it be dishonest to take it if you knew whoever sold those picture frames didn't know it was in there?

<question>



Can I get in trouble for not stopping a fight ?

<answer>

<answer from>

<question from>



boomakin...

</question from>



<question>



My company is ignoring my demand letter for wrong termination...should i file a compant with the MCAD if a Law?



</question>



<answer from>



mattyboy...
Member since: 27 June 2008
Total points: 4249 (Level 4)



</answer from>



<answer>



Most states are employment at will, meaning an employer can legally fire you any time for any reason, there for there would be no such thing as "wrongful termination" in those states.  If your state is one of them, they're ignoring you because you have no case.



</answer>



<answer from>



Michael C
Member since: 18 June 2007
Total points: 9653 (Level 5)



</answer from>



<answer>



Mattyboy, even in at will employment states there can be grounds for wrongful termination. There are still federally protected classes that can not be the basis for termination (sex, race, religion, etc).



If you think you really have a case and the company is not responding, you need to see a lawyer or report to your state's labor board.



</answer>



<answer from>



Amanda D
Member since: 10 September 2008
Total points: 385 (Level 2)



</answer from>



<answer>



What was the reason they gave for termination?  You can file a complaint but you will probably not get anywhere unless you were employed under a contract (for a specific amount of time/ until the completion of a specific job), or for any discrimination reason (religion, race, disability, sex, ect.).  If you were termination with or without reason from the company, and none of these stipulations apply, you are probably better off just cutting your losses and looking for a new job.



</answer>

<question from>



Roarks
Member since: 01 October 2008
Total points: 95 (Level 1)



</question from>



<question>



Going back to the law after 10 years off?



I graduated Law School and passed the bar in 1999.  I've been in a different field for the past 10 years.  I've paid my state and American Bar dues every year but have neglected CLE.  I'm interested in practicing law at this point, but not sure if this is too big of an uphill battle to gain competence and get caught up on CLE.  If anyone has any experience in this, I'd appreciate some input.



</question>



<answer from>
Member since: 18 December 2007
Total points: 1329 (Level 3)



</answer from>



<answer>



Sorry, no idea...  Just wanted to say Good Luck - I hope that everything goes brilliantly.



</answer>



<answer from>



raichasa...
Member since: 12 May 2007
Total points: 48585 (Level 7)



Contributing In:



Law & Ethics



</answer from>



<answer>



The basic principles of law you learned in school haven't changed since 1999, only the specific application.  Law school doesn't teach you much about the specific applications anyway.  I'd say that you are pretty much frozen in time and on a par with newbies coming out of law school today.



So, in my firm that would qualify you for an entry level position where you would start with simpler stuff until you gained experience with the specific applications of law needed in the area you are practicing and until you learn more about civil procedure for your area.  (Civil procedure and court rules do change a lot over time.)



The problem for you is finding that position, especially if you are trying to find a law job that will pay as much as the field you have been in for 10 years.



But I think it would be unwise to try to go out on your own.  Without a mentor, you will need to spend hours and hours per week looking up what you need to know.  So much easier to have someone to point you in the right direction.



Good luck.



</answer>

sophist
Member since: 24 May 2006
Total points: 43570 (Level 7)



Contributing In:



Philosophy



Government



Law & Ethics



</answer from>



<answer>



Feel very fortunate. When I was in school we AVERAGED 35 students per class. I have been in classes with 50 other students. My chem classes did not drop until I was in college and then we had about 30 students.



The number of students is controlled by necessity. Their is a limited number of classrooms in any district. The school system must fit whatever number of students they have into those classrooms.



In high school you do have the option of choosing when to take a class. The more popular time slots will have more students in them. You might want to consider rescheduling some of your classes.

"We just can't find enough teachers"



"We didn't anticipate the number who wanted to take chemistry, we are doing the best we can."



The only possible illegality would be if your school was dominated by one ethnic group and a school with another pattern had smaller classes (over all probably, not just one class), and then a federal suit might be resolved about the time you finished college.

<question from>



SharpDr0...

</question from>



<question>



Ownership of the advertising campaign?



When an advertising agency creates an ad or a whole campaign, who owns the rights to that? Is it the property of the agency or their client?



</question>



</answer from>



<answer>



It is the client who owns the property. The agency surrenders their ownership by virtue of a sale. The only thing the agency can claim thereafter is the credit of authorship. Which is a simple fact.



</answer>

<answer>



If you do it very carefully.
1) attach it to an email addressed to yourself.



2) print out the email and make a certified photocopy immediately.
3) Keep it attached to your original design.
4) when you post it, state that the item is copyrighted using the copyright symbol, your name and the date of the email along with the statement "All rights reserved".



This will not stop piracy, but will give you third party testament to your copyright and is valid in any court.

Sounds like a bogus argument to mean, or at best, an overprotective interpretation of HIPAA. Juan Moore Beer
Member since: 10 July 2006
Total points: 2514 (Level 4)

</answer>



<answer from>



poor_yor...
Member since: 26 April 2007
Total points: 140 (Level 1)



</answer from>



<answer>



It depends. Most restraining orders are temporary restraining orders. They can mature into permanent injunctions, but there has to be a pretty big showing of potential harm to do that. Being temporary, they last up to a year before there needs to be showing of continuing harm to get it extended or made into a permanent injunction. That being said, when you are 18, the TRO will probably have lapsed anyway. This is all assuming that your mother can get the TRO in the first place. You can have a hearing, and the judge will hear both sides of whether there should be a temporary restraining order.



Now this guy that you are seeing, make sure that you don't engage in any sorts of sexual acts, because that would constitute statutory rape, and I'm sure that you don't want to be responsible for sending him to jail.



All in all, you should listen to your mother, and wait until you are 18.



</answer>



<answer from>



rickinno...
Member since: 13 May 2007
Total points: 39496 (Level 7)



Contributing In:



Law & Ethics



</answer from>



<answer>



"i have been seeing this 22 year old boy"



First off, a 22 year old is NOT a boy - he is an adult man, and has NO business whatsoever hanging around a 16 year old kid. Listen to your Mom. There are *ONLY* two reasons that a 22 year old man would want to date a 16 year old child. One is that he's not capable of getting a date with an adult closer to his own age, and the other is that he believes he can take advantage of your youth and immaturity to get sex. That's it - there are NO other reasons.



Only a court can issue a restraining order. Only the same court can remove it. If your Mom asks the court to issue an order, and they do, then it can only be taken off by the court - your turning 18 does not affect the order - although at 18 you could go to the court and ask them to remove it as it pertains to you.



Since you are a minor, however, your Mom can, herself, simply order him not to have contact with you, and he is required by law to comply. That personal order from your Mom will expire when you turn 18.



Richard



</answer>

<answer from>



bcnu
Member since: 04 June 2007
Total points: 3747 (Level 4)



Contributing In:



Law & Ethics



</answer from>



<answer>



Freedom of speech, limited by incitement of violence. Offensive speech by one group should not generally be restricted by legal action by another. Flag is a symbol with subjective interpretation (proud history, horrendous crimes), so limiting its use would deprive some people of their right to a proud display, even if others see it differently. The flag itself is not dangerous. Limitations on free speech must endure strict scrutiny under the constitution and must be carefully crafted to assert only the minimum infringement necessary to carry out a bona fide goal for the benefit of the public (such as maintaining community peace). The flag (among ALL flags) could be limited as to time or place for its display, but not effectively banned, nor singled out because of its content (except in comparison, perhaps, with the American flag, which must always be higher than other flags, etc).



</answer>

<question>



Euthanasia why should it be legal?



tell me in your opinion if euthanasia is correct for th elderly who are in pain



</question>



<answer from>



Katherine S
Member since: 06 October 2007
Total points: 1162 (Level 3)



</answer from>



<answer>



<evaluation>Best Answer - Chosen by Asker</evaluation>



YES!



Firstly, let me say that I am completely biased. My grad-dad died a long, slow and very painful death from lung cancer due to asbestos.  He should not have been made to suffer for 3 long months in pain that the morphine could not cover.  In  the end he did not know who he was, who his family were and just lay there and cried wanting the pain to end. It is simple - He wanted the pain to stop, he should have had that control.



The problem from a legal point of view would be at what point and for what disease should people be allowed to make these decisions.  This should be the debate, not if it should be allowed. I can see the problems in who gets to make the decision - the patient who may have lost the ability to make decisions for themselves, families and the conflict this may cause or the law - which takes time and is already overburdened.  The idea of for what reason/diseases is also interesting - for depression or for end stage cancer?



There are no easy answers but there should be a discussion rather than ignoring the issue.



</answer>

Gerry K

Total points: 99 (Level 1)



</question from>



<question>



How do I file for a copyright?



for song lyrics



</question>



</answer from>



<answer>



You need to prove that you created that song on a certain date.  And on that date the music was automatically copyrighted. Put your music or song in some tangible form - a piece of paper, a CD, a floppy disk or written as sheet music or even on a napkin. Just remember - it HAS to be in a tangible form. Then you have to find a copyright office, google it with your state and city, and they will give you a process to go through.



</answer>

<answer from>



TOON ARMY FOR EVER.
Member since: 19 June 2007
Total points: 5647 (Level 5)



</answer from>



<answer>



Do I need a TV Licence?



You need a TV Licence to use any television receiving equipment such as a TV set, digital box, DVD or video recorder, PC, laptop or mobile phone to watch or record television programmes as they're being shown on TV.



If you use a digital box with a hi-fi system or another device that can only be used to produce sounds and can't display TV programmes, and you don't install or use any other TV receiving equipment, you don't need a TV Licence.



Where does it state that your licence is for watching the BBC only?It is for all tv channels including Sky/Virgin/Free Sat.



Source(s):



http://www.tvlicensing.co.uk/information...



</answer>

alisiaba...

</answer from>

<answer from>



GCOP
Member since: 17 August 2007
Total points: 519 (Level 2)



</answer from>



<answer>



No, you need to have your high school diploma. A lot of departments are requiring at least 60 college hours or even a degree. Good luck!



</answer>



<answer from>



Mattybea...
Member since: 01 October 2008
Total points: 98 (Level 1)



</answer from>



<answer>



It would be dependent on the hiring qualifications of the department you're looking at.



Most departments will want a high school diploma OR EQUIVALENT, as well as college hours.  The usual requirement is anywhere from 35 college hours up to a full 4-year bachelors degree.  The college is the important part, not so much the GED.  As you can see from the link attached in the sources, the Illinois State Police require college, and no mention is made of exclusions for those applicants who hold a GED rather than a diploma.



As a former police officer who has a GED and a college degree, I can't say that my education prior to college was a sticking point.  I knew of several officers who were hired with ONLY a high school equivalency and completion of a private, state-licensed police academy, although they were employed by smaller towns whose hiring and firing policies and decisions were made only by the police chief and possibly the town council.



Look into the licensing/certification requirements for your state, or call your state police recruiter and ask-- they'll give you the right information right off the bat and even suggest where you could start heading education-wise if you really want to go into law enforcement.



Hope this answer is helpful to you.



Source(s):



Professional experience;



http://www.illinoistrooper.com/requireme...



</answer>

<question from>



Ben
Member since: 22 July 2008
Total points: 84 (Level 1)



</question from>



<question>



What are the roles of the courts?



I want to know the differences between a lawyer, barrister, solicitor and judge = ]



</question>



</answer from>



<answer>



A barrister is a person who is licensed to prepare and try cases in court. A solicitor is a person who is licensed to handle out-of-court legal matters. These terms are English in origin. A lawyer is a person who is licensed to handle both. It is an American term. A judge is a person who decides contested matters. The judge serves as the trier of fact and the trier of law when there is no jury. The judge serves only as the trier of law when there is a jury, which serves as the trier of fact.



</answer>

<answer from>



Kris_the...
Member since: 17 August 2008
Total points: 233 (Level 1)



</answer from>



<answer>



The mandatory break law in New Jersey only applies to minors under the age of 18 and they must be given a thirty (30) minute meal period after five (5) consecutive hours of work. Company policy dictates break and lunch periods for anyone over the age of 18.



So I'm afraid to tell you that your company policy is the final word.



Hope this helps.



Source(s):



http://lwd.dol.state.nj.us/labor/wagehou...



</answer>

<question>



Is this an actual law firm or collection agency?



My friend had a debt collection from 2004 sent to a law firm called Hayt, Hayt, and Landau in Florida. When she first was contacted by them, they said they were attorneys of her original creditor (credit acceptance, I think). She is a student, and makes very little money, so she said she could only afford to pay $40 month. The lady told her "that's way too little". So my friend said "well thats all I can afford". Next thing she knows, about 6 months later, she gets a letter to appear in court. She has graduated from college but has not found a better job, but gets a few more hours at work and agreed to pay $100 to keep from getting her wages garnished. She settled this at the courthouse on that court date. The "attorneys" weren't there, and they entered this over the phone.
She has been paying this $100 faithfully, but with her only making $550 a month, it is KILLING her to pay this. She has went 1 month already almost losing electricity. My questions are, what can she do, and is this REALLY a law firm, or a collection agency? Also, they don't send receipts, or report her paying to the credit bureaus. So the only proof she has of paying is her money order receipts.



</question>



<answer from>



LovesThe...
Member since: 20 July 2008
Total points: 4043 (Level 4)



Contributing In:



Law & Ethics



</answer from>



<answer>



<evaluation>Best Answer - Chosen by Asker</evaluation>



Hayt, Hayt, and Landau is a law firm acting as a collection agency.  They operate in Florida and in other states.



Do a Yahoo! search on that name.  You will have interesting results.



She may consult a counselor at her credit union, if she is a member.



She should be very concerned about getting no receipt or statement.



She may want to contact the consumer protection division of the state attorney general's office to see if she can find any help there.



Overwhelming debt at such a young age limits one's options.



Source(s):



30 years experience in civil litigation



</answer>

nosle.com todd.more
nanucbe



Source(s):



cause and effect

<answer from>



knitting...
Member since: 01 February 2008
Total points: 2478 (Level 3)



</answer from>



<answer>



I would use the term "person with a hearing impairment" if at all possible. It's more in agreement with the standards of "people first" language.



Rather than specify that the person does not speak, I would simply note the means that the person does use, ie. ASL, keyboard, etc. (There are many technological means to produce speech.)



Therefore, your "deaf mute" might be "a woman with a hearing impairment who communicates primarily with ASL." Note that she's a person first, not a disability.



Source(s):



MA, Special Education



</answer>

<question>



What is happening with HR 5843, the bill to legalize responsible use and possession of marijuana?



what is its status? Do you support it?



</question>



<answer from>



Michael C
Member since: 18 June 2007
Total points: 9644 (Level 5)



</answer from>



<answer>



The bill was introduced in April and appears to have gone nowhere since then. I would be amazed if it got very far.



Source(s):



http://www.govtrack.us/congress/bill.xpd...



</answer>



<answer from>



Pfo
Member since: 11 December 2006
Total points: 48966 (Level 7)



</answer from>



<answer>



Never heard of this until now, Barney Frank is proposing it.  Not surprising, he probably needs to smoke it after his debacles with Fannie Mae and Freddie Mac policy (look it up, he's one of the main guys responsible).



After looking at it, it really doesn't legalize it:



http://www.house.gov/frank/hr5843summary...



It removes federal penalties, state penalties still exist.
It decriminalizes possession and not-for-profit transfer, you still can't sell it.
Public use is still punished, a $100 fine.
Growing it is still illegal.



It was introduced, status pending, hasn't made it to committee:
http://www.govtrack.us/congress/bill.xpd...



</answer>

It's all relative.

<answer>



Your question mixes up two separate issues. A legal sanction to kill is a codified statute enacted as law which permits the taking of life(ie:the death penalty) A moral imperative to kill is an ethical judgement of an individual or group of individuals unsanctioned by law. You can, in retrospect, judge the removal of a particularly heinous individual like Hitler or Stalin early in their regimes would have saved countless lives and therefore would have been ethically and morally justified but if you were caught in the act in the early 20's you would have been regarded as a murderer, assasin and terrorist because neither Hitler nor Stalin were guilty of anything yet.



</answer>



<answer from>

Morally right and legally right are two different things.

Diana B
Member since: 17 May 2007
Total points: 1664 (Level 3)



</answer from>



<answer>



"Is it moral to kill someone when you have seen them carry out a crime that would influence so many people that their death would help prevent the deaths of hundreds of others?"

Does Yahoo Answers have a policy against rheotical questions?  You know, the ones people ask when they're not really interested in an answer?  Oh well - anyway, as ypu've learned by now, there is a difference between morality and the law.  Not that the law is immoral, but that you set your own morality and hope that the law supports it.  This question is also poorly phrased because it seems to suggest that the critical events are both past (you've seen them carry out the crime) and future (the witnessed crime would influence something in the future), and also blends certainty (the witnessed crime definitely happened) with the uncertain (would influence).  The question is also so factually vague that it presents no adequate basis for a response.  I'd liek to say that having witnessed the death of Mary Joe Koepechne (okay, so I just saw a documentary about it) I should kill some Kennedys - that would cause an effect damn possible to save a bunch of lives...maybe.

"Would it look good in the eyes of the press to snuff out the life of a really sick person if their death meant that so many thousands would live?"

Why does everybody constantly assume that there's one press?  Answer - questions of morality always lose their ambiguity when there's a chance that the media may actually be on your side.  In your case, the "New York Post" (depending on the "victim") might find this mercy-murder good-in-the-eyes, while the "New York Times" might not.

"Hitler for instance. His early death wouldve meant life for millions. yet would the press tolerate his death when he was a budding politician?"

Was Hitler ever a budding anything?  For one thing, you evince no understanding for the state of Germany during Hitler's early political years.  Nobody would have had a tear for anybdy.  Of course nobody would ahve felt the same not knowing of the future because....well, nobody knew of the future.  However, knowing the future leaves a rather unpalatable dillemma - sure, kill Hitler for stuff he doesn;t even know about; but that means that the killer himself would be subjecting himself to punishment for things he doesn't even know he will do.

Oh, as everybody knows, Hitler didn;t start WWII, he had plenty of help.  One man doesn;t alone start a war that kills millions.  Also, in the 60+ years since the end of the war, popular culture has seen tons of alternate-history fiction about the war alone; what if Hitler was a nice guy, what if he died before he was famous, what if they won the war, etc.  Remember, if you think that your killing f Hitler will change things you're taking responsibility for every change, for better or worse.  Doesn't sound so easy now does, it?

"Even weeks before hitler ordered the invasion of french germany the West was blind to him but many knew his dangers."

How can the West be blind, yet know his dangers?  Are you talking of wilfull blindness or just stupidity?  The West knew about Hitler's plans to conquer Eastern Europe and for the most part, they didn;t really care.  If anybody was surprised - it was Hitler:  Britain and France uniting against a fellow non-Bolshevik country to save...Poland.  Nobody much cared because in pre-war Europe, the real danger was not a fascist dictatorship which seemed generally adored by its own citizens, but a communist police state in perpetual state of Stalinist purge.  People weren't afraid of Nazis in pre-war Europe - most countries already were Nazi.

"why is mankind so stupid in the face of evil, snuff it out, kill it when you have the chance."

Isn;t this the part where the camera pans to Rod Serling, and we learn how the horrible, evil fate that the righteou skiller sought o proevent was actually caused by him?



</answer>



<answer from>



Kit Fang
Member since: 12 February 2007
Total points: 5404 (Level 5)

<question from>



Tarquin McConnor, MP
Member since: 19 June 2008
Total points: 311 (Level 2)



</question from>



<question>



Why does Society rewards roguery by feeding and housing the scoundrels with Tax-payer money...?



when its a lot most efficient and inexpensive hanging them from every tree branch, lamp post, tower, and building in the city, before setting their body ablaze, and using the ashes a fertiliser?



</question>



<additional details>



No, I don't think we should kill off religious and ethnic minorities, quite the contrary actually. But I do think, we don't need to waste money housing and feeding the common vagabond, when we can just kill 'em off, and scare the potential rogue away from crime.



</additional details>



<answer from>



jigokusa...
Member since: 06 February 2006
Total points: 1243 (Level 3)



</answer from>



<answer>



<evaluation>Best Answer - Chosen by Asker</evaluation>



Executing thieves and fraudsters is.... excessive to say the least. Here's why it doesn't work:

1. You run into enough instances of faulty convictions with the current set of capital crimes. If you throw every thief onto death-row, then the number is going to increase exponentially.

2. The expense of capital punishment comes largely from the appeals that ensue. We don't want to execute innocent people, so we cannot simply get rid of the appeals process.

3. Nothing says that the specter of punishment is enough to deter thieves. No one WANTS to go to jail, and so no one who commits crime is doing so with the idea that they're going to be caught. The idea of punishment as a deterrent is not one that has a demonstrable basis.



So, it's not cheaper, a lot of innocent people will die, and there's no basis for believing that it would be a deterrent.



Source(s):



If jail was just some place where people got free meals and shelter... then we would not have a homeless problem.



</answer>

<answer from>



Kevin B
Member since: 27 December 2007
Total points: 792 (Level 2)



</answer from>



<answer>



I don't think you've given a logical arguement for reducing the drinking age either...In fact you're using "argumentum ad populum" and "argumentum ad hominem" to boast a "non sequitur" claim that one's self determination is no more greater than one's right to drink when clearly self determination is more important.



Saying "The government deems me responsible enough to vote" means "Therefore I should be allowed to drink too" is simply "argumentum verbosium" offering no proof that a society would benefit better by having younger drinkers rather than younger voters.



The problem with "argumentum verbosium" is that anything is right so long as you say it is...

EXAMPLE "The government deems me responsible enough to join an armed service, therefore I should be allowed to start my own armed militia"

So anyone who runs their mouth is right.

Hopefully this shows you that "Logic" and "Politics" (Popular or Otherwise) do not always mix.



Source(s):



http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fallacy



</answer>



<answer from>



Dell
Member since: 30 July 2008

<question>



I received a letter with a statement of account +a notice before proceeding under the county courts act. ?



on the envelope of the letter there was this words printed on it (DO NOT DISCARD-Notice prior to proceedings under the County Courts Act) is this illegal or not to me is invasion of privacy am i wrong?



</question>



</answer from>



<answer>



Not illegal as such but if I were you I would complain to the Office of Fair Trading who have jurisdiction over debt collctors.People are entitled if not obliged to warn you that they propose to issue a County Court summons.But the state of your account with however is claiming the money is between you and them not the Postman or anyone else in your home who might read it.I doubt if you will find the words "Notice Before Proceeding....." in the County Court Acts.It is a phrase made up by debt collectors to get your attention.

<question>



Does anyone know the guidelines and timescales for this?



I have been off work for 18 months on long term sick leave. I know its probably dependent on the company but I have heard that after a certain amount of time you can get paid off on medical grounds. I have been paid out on a company pension scheme that pays me part of my salary



</question>



</answer from>



<answer>



There are no statutory rules on this.  The answer will be contained in your contract and the terms and conditions governing long-term sickness.
Dependent on your age, you may be getting paid under a PHI scheme, not your pension. The t&c's of the scheme under which you are paid are contained in your contract.
If it looks like you'll not be retuning for some time, the PHI scheme operator (which is not your employer) may offer to buy you out with a lump sum.

But to reiterate: there is no law requiring them to do so.

I'm a lawyer

<question>



When if at all is a criminal conviction spent?



9 years ago i was convicted of possession of a very smell amount of Ecstasy. I know this will be on record for life but somebody told me that after 10 years it's a spent conviction and for most jobs  you don't have to declare it.

Is this true that after a period of time I don't have to declare this on job applications?

<additional details>

<answer from>



rickinno...
Member since: 13 May 2007
Total points: 35182 (Level 7)



Contributing In:



Law & Ethics



</answer from>



<answer>



Depends on the laws of your unknown country and/or State, and you didn't tell us where you are.

EDIT - Under the UK "Rehabilitation of Offenders Act", a conviction that results only in a fine is spent after five years from the date of conviction.

Even if an employer asks "Have you ever been convicted.....?" you can legally answer "no", and it is a violation of law for the employer to investigate spent convictions, or use them to make hiring decisions.

Richard



</answer>

<answer from>



laughter...

<answer>

</answer>



<answer from>



Bardic
Member since: 21 November 2006
Total points: 39845 (Level 7)



</answer from>



<answer>



In the UK a sentence of a fine is considered "spent" after 5 years.  Once a conviction is ‘spent’, the convicted person does not have to reveal it or admit its existence in most circumstances.  However, there are some exceptions relating to employment and these are listed in the Exceptions order to the Rehabilitiation of Offenders Act.  The two main exceptions relate to working with children or working with the elderly or sick people.  If a person wants to apply for a position that involves working with children or working with the elderly or sick people they are required to reveal all convictions, both spent and unspent.

Hope that helps.



</answer>

<question from>



mental blank
Member since: 01 October 2008
Total points: 95 (Level 1)



</question from>



<question>



Third party contract law case?



A had heard from B, his brother, that he had offered C $160,000 for the vase. He immediately went to C's home with $170,000 with the intention of buying the vase for himself. He gave the money to C's wife, who said to A that C was not at home but she would let him know that A had bought the vase. A took the vase away with him.
What case law supports that A is entitled to the vase? Does anyone know of a past law case that is similar to this situation?



</question>



</answer from>



<answer>



Look for intentional interference with contracts or business relationships. There will be cases where B and C do not have a "contract" and those where B and C have a "business relationship" not amounting to contract but possibly giving them rights against meddlers.



However, this is done all the time. If I make an offer to buy your house for $160,000 and tell my brother before you accept, he can certainly make a higher offer and the seller can accept any higher offer until there is a contract acceptance, after which there would be damages for breach.



</answer>

<answer>



<evaluation>Best Answer - Chosen by Asker</evaluation>



&gt; 2) It's murder - In which case I would respond "Scientifically speaking, a fetus is not alive until 24 weeks, so it can't be killed"

I am on your side, but this is a flawed argument.

the issue is not when the fetus is alive in a scientific sense, and anyway science offers no answers because the question diesn;t really make sense - life happens on a continuum and happens in various forms, ranging (for humans at least) from gametes, to fetuses, to those who have been born but not yet died, to those who have died while at any of the other stages.

the issue is solely a legal one - in our system of laws, which, as you noted, are to be strictly interpreted in a secular fashion, one does not achieve the standing of "personhood" until one is born alive. during the period after birth, and while still alive, you are a legal person, before that and after, "not so much" as the kids like to say :)

we can quibble about when someone dies in fuzzy cases, but the exception just proves the rule.

same for birth, you can quibble about when the precise moment birth occurs, and at a fine enough scale, these transitions happen on a continuum of their own, but they do happen. we are not really debating where the actual instant of birth is when determining personhood.

similarly for the definition for "alive".

if any of these are in dispute in a particular case, then courts are prepared to listen to the evidence and decide. That happens sometime, although more with death then birth.

Bottom line, if you have not been born alive yet, then you are a human being still at the fetus stage of your lifecycle, and have no legal standing under our system as a person.

there is no serious legal argument to be made that it should be otherwise. really, someone would suggest that a fetus, from the stage it is only 2 cells, has the same rights and responsibilities as a citizen? I don't think anyone is going to argue that.

The real issue is that people are not clear on what it means to keep their religious  beliefs, which they are entitled to hold,  out of a secular legal system that needs to treat others with different or no religious beliefs fairly and equally.

I am not sure if that is deliberate ignorance, or for some reason is hard to grasp among those brought up in certain religions, but either way, it seems to be the case. Sad really,



</answer>

<question>



Is there anything unethical about?



Ethics, are these examples actually wrong for any reason? If so WHY?
An ethical question: What part of a child sneaking a cookie from the cookie jar is morally wrong? The sneaking is necessary because they know that their parents don't want them to eat it. But is is morally wrong? And if so why? If it's a two year old taking the cookie is this the same as a 12 year old taking it? I was thinking about this today, a child gets everything s/he has from their parents, in most countries it's a legal requirement to look after your child (although I'm aware that legality and morality are not always on the same page). In essence, what's yours is your child's as well. Originally I was trying to make a point with this but the more I thought about it the less sure I was. What is wrong about taking the cookie? If it's simply because your mom or dad told you not to I might ask then is it morally wrong for a well endowed daughter to wear a low cut top after being told no, or for a young man who gets his ear pierced when told not to? I don't see any ethical implications with any of these things. Just parents with power forcing their will on their children. Can someone who does explain to me why they do? The more detailed the better, thanks everyone!



</question>



<additional details>



"Are you saying that you think you should be able to do anything you want as long as you're sneaky enough not to get caught? " No, no I'm not, swing and a miss!



</additional details>



<answer from>



Keith
Member since: 23 September 2006
Total points: 5653 (Level 5)



</answer from>



<answer>



<evaluation>Best Answer - Chosen by Asker</evaluation>



I don't believe there isa question of an ethical issue until the child knows the difference between right and wrong.  Does a 2 year old really understand the difference between right and wrong?  Maybe not.  Does a 12 year old? Probably.  My question would be is it really an ethical issue when it concerns a child taking a cookie from their home cookie jar?



</answer>



<asker's comment>



Well said.



</asker's comment>



<answer from>



Concerne...
Member since: 30 June 2006
Total points: 17676 (Level 6)



</answer from>



<answer>



Are you saying that you think you should be able to do anything you want as long as you're sneaky enough not to get caught?  Sorry, but I can't agree.  That's the kind of rationalization criminals have always used to justify everything from robbery to murder.  We need laws and rules to maintain social order, and learning to obey them begins in the home in early childhood.  If a child wants a cookie and asks his parents for it, they'll probably give it to him.  If he takes it after being told he can't have it, it shows he can't be trusted and he should face the consequences.  It's not just about power; it's about knowing when not to cross the line between proper and improper behavior, or between honesty and dishonesty.



</answer>



<answer from>



Kyle_wre...
Member since: 17 October 2007
Total points: 177 (Level 1)



</answer from>



<answer>



I am actually agreeing with you on this one.  Yes, parents need to assert their authority to let their kids know who is in charge.  In the cookie jar incident, the parents are "testing/trusting" the kid as to what the kid will do when they are not looking.  From this standpoint, the parents have every right to say, "no, you cannot have a cookie".  When or if the kid does steal the cookie and get away with it, that only implies that if you are sneaky enough, you can get whatever you want.  If you know much about life, then you would know that it is pretty easy to get caught up in a web of lies and fall face flat.  So, in conclusion to the cookie problem, no, it is not morally/ethically correct to steal the cookie.
In the case of the "well endowed daughter and the low cut top", she has the maturity to make that decision at a certain age.  Until then, the advice of her parents is all she has to go by, considering she has had little to no experience with flaunting her body.  There is a time when the daughter needs to draw the line, though, and take charge of her life.
Finally, in the case of the "young man who gets his ear pierced when told not to", there should be no argument there.  The young man has every right to get his ear pierced.  His decision would affect no one but himself, so if he decides to pierce his ear, then the parents should have no problem with it.  But as experience has taught, parents rarely act that way.  They try to live their lives through their kids because it's the only way they can be kids again.  Whether it's sports, academics, or restrictions, parents usually try to complete what they did not accomplish in their youth through their children.

I sincerely hope this has helped clear your head,
-Kyle



</answer>

<question>



Is there a legal way of finding out whether someone has a criminal record?  Conviction was in 1970's in South?



My ex-partner has made false allegations against me, and used entrapment.  I have no criminal record, not even a parking violation. Her reason is greed, and to avoid paying a large sum of money she owes to me.  She alleges harassment, without violence. She is very convincing, and the authorities have chosen to believe her so far, although I believe their opinion will change when evidence they are not aware of becomes common knowledge. She alleged the same three years ago, about a neighbour, but this is inadmissable.  She is stringing it out (three cancellations already) to cause me anxiety.
My brief asked me if she has a criminal record.  It is possible she has, but I don't know how to find out. She told me she travelled on the train at weekends between Kingston, Surrey Hospital and Brighton I think, between 1968 and 1972 to see her boyfriend who lived on the south coast. She only bought a ticket to cover the last stop of the journey, but was caught by the Railway Police, and convicted of travelling for at least a year on the trains without paying.  She was fined and received a warning.  Also, she told me she gave evidence at Court 2 of the Old Bailey between 1977 and 1980, (she told me she had lied) in support of her new boyfriend, Frank.  Frank was convicted of drug dealing and sentenced to four years, but she maintained he was a recreational user who bought a little extra to supply to his friends. The Police raided his house, but after his arrest, and unbeknown to them, my ex-Partner told me she recovered the money from his drug sales from his house at his request, and kept it for him. 
She may still have kept her first partner's surname at that time, so I am beginning to accept that details of that trial may be impossible to find especially now she has reverted to her maiden name.  Is there anyone out there, (a criminal lawyer, a criminal records keeper, anyone who knows the system, and) who could help me find the impossible?  Thank you for reading this, and your help may save me.



</question>



<answer from>



emanwelg...
Member since: 09 May 2008
Total points: 1424 (Level 3)



Contributing In:



Law & Ethics



</answer from>



<answer>



<evaluation>Best Answer - Chosen by Asker</evaluation>



I'm assuming that you are facing a criminal trial, and that this isn't about your civil action for debt against her.
A minor offence committed in the late 60s/early 70s will be spent for the purposes of the Rehabilitation of Offenders Act which will make it much harder to find. That would also be inadmissible, prima facie, but together with the previous false accusations it might be used to establish a propensity to lie, which might make it admissible. 
Really, you should trust your lawyer on this. He is more likely to be able to judge admissibility, and find out about previous convictions, than you are, privately. You could give evidence that she lied in a criminal trial in the late 70s... but whether or not it would help (or be admissible as evidence of bad character) is, again dubious.



Good luck with it anyway.



Source(s):



Law Graduate - Not Qualified Lawyer



</answer>

<answer from>



Azuriel8...
Member since: 01 October 2008
Total points: 127 (Level 1)



</answer from>



<answer>



For starters, just tell the judge the truth: that you don't have insurance, because you simply can't afford to pay for it.  Judges are people too, and considering the cost of going through law school these days ...



But make it clear that you are not currently driving your car, it is inside you garage, locked up, and will not be driven by yourself or anyone else until you do have insurance.  The court will set a date for this to happen by, agree to it sincerely, and make sure you get the insurance before that date.  If you tell the truth, the court will generally be sympathetic the first time round, but if there's a second time, will generally give a very harsh judgment.



With some luck, you might even get off without the fine, or a delayed fine of sorts if you don't have insurance by the agreed date.



Source(s):



working as a lawyer



</answer>

<answer>



This may not be what you are looking for, but...



What state do you live in?  The facts are simple.  If the girl was under the age of consent (in many states it is 18), and your friend was legally an adult, then he committed statutory rape.  It has nothing to do with her father being a prick or who he knows, as much as it sucks for your friend.  If this was the case, he was not wrongfully accused.



Anyone charged with a sex crime must register as a sex offender, it's the law.  And as far as the movie, he would know what would be a violation of his parole.  The police would not be doing a "raid" on his house without a warrant for a very good reason.



No offense, but either you don't have all the facts or this story is greatly exaggerated.  If he was in state prison he was convicted of a felony... which means he went through the court system at a federal level, not a city level/district court.  In other words, it's impossible that the girl's father had that amount of pull to get him "wrongly convicted."  The police do not do the prosecuting, so the dad's business being across from the police is irrelevant... even if he was dating the prosecutor, it wouldn't matter- the prosecutor's job is just that, to prosecute!  As far as the psychologists... seriously we are to believe that the word one one opposed to five kept him in jail?



I understand you want to help your friend, but there is no conspiracy of the justice system here, just a lot of BS.



Source(s):



Studying law & my common sense

<question>



Criminal Law Questions I don't understand, of find in text..?



Hello all. Fist semester and in the 8th week. Doing pretty well but there are some questions at the end on each chapter to study on to help w/ the exams. Here are out of 8 chapters (Over 160 Questions) I don't get/understand fully or didn't find in the text. Any help would be great. This is not for an exam, this is some questions from the study guide on each chapter to help me w/ my study guide. Know many of them, and found a lot out of 160 questions. Thought I would give the good 'ol yahoo community a shot at a few that knows the law. Thanks for any help. Exam is in 2-week's. Please don't get me wrong these are not exam questions, but chapter questions from the back of the book. "Criminal Law", Ninth Edition, by: Joel Samaha. New to this state and college, and kind of don't know anyone here yet. Trying to meet friends, but most want to party all the time & partying is cool, but my education is more important. Thank's all, never tried yahoo much, so hope to get some good honest answers. Thank you all, will vote for best. 1. The process by which lower courts base their opinions and decisions upon previous opinions and decisions of higher appellate courts is:



a. Habeas Corpus



b. Stare Decisis



c. Corpus Delicti



d. Nunc Pro Tunc
