<answer>



I do agree with the other posters, but I am bored and you spent a great deal of time typing so I will answer.



1.  The process by which lower courts base their opinion on prior appellate decisions is Stare Decis (latin for "stand the decision")  The other answers are all procedural.  Habeas Corpus is post-conviction relief for people held improperly.  Nunc pro tunc allows lawyers to doctor the books, and back date stuff with the court's permission.  Corpus delicti is the total body of evidence of a crime.



2. Unconscious automatism is an affirmative defense.  It allows a defendant to be relieved of criminal responsibility for an action over which he/she literally had no control.



3.  Dominion and Control is the answer.  They are the legal elements of posession.  You will cover it when you take property.



4. Intent required, in addition to the act, is specific or general intent.  Specific intent correlates to either purposeful actions or knowledgeable actions under the Model Penal Code.  General intent covers recklessness and negligence.  The other answers are distractors, Transferred intent is a way, in limited circumstances, of establishing Mens Rea for a crime.  Strict liability is a small class of crimes for which no intent need be proven, only the action.



5.  The right to privacy is found in Mapp v. Ohio, where the Supreme first extended the exclusion doctrine under the 4th amendment to the states.  However, the answer you are looking for is Griswold v. Connecticut, since the court essentially erected a right to privacy from the 3, 4, 5, 9, 10, 14th amendments.



6. The crime drama with only women who speak to the boss through a microphone is Charlie's Angels.



7. Failure to protect is an omission crime which sets forth an affirmative duty to protect and punishes any failure.



8.  Probably Actual and Sole posession since the automobile is probably locked and no one else has access to it.  However, if he were at work, not in in close proximity it would be Contructive and Sole posession of it.



9.  The infamous equal protection clause is in the 14th amendment, right after the Due process clause.



10.  Crimes for which intent need not be proven are called strict liability crimes, or in older terms Absolute liability crimes.



11. A criminal statute not clear to a person of reasonable intellect is a violation of the Vagueness doctrine requiring that people be on notice of what conduct constitutes a crime.  Based on the fill in the blank spaces the actual term you want is the Due Process clause.  For more info look up procedural due process as opposed to substantive due process.



12. A law retroactively making conduct illegal is invalid under the ex post facto clause.  (Latin for "after the fact")



13. Conduct that is evil only because it is prohibited is Malum prohibitum (latin for evil because of prohibition).



14. Conduct that is evil by nature is Malum in se. (Latin for evil by its very existence.  A related latin phrase you will become familiar with is Per se.)



15. Stare Decis does NOT require that judges follow legislative history, only prior judicial decisions.



16.  Culpability IS based on blameworthyness.



17.  Trick question.  You must answer false.  All material elements must be proven BEYOND A REASONABLE DOUBT rather than mere probable cause or preponderance.



18.  False, the only thing which strict liability crimes do require is the actus reus.  What you don't need is mens rea.



19.  True, omission is the failure to act upon a legally imposed duty.



20.  Mere presence can never be a crime.  There is no action, just passive presence.  Moreover there is no mens rea, only apathy.



21.  D.  automtism is an affirmative defense which attacks the voluntariness of the actus reus.  It is NOT a level of culpabilty.  It essentially says "yes the action happened, yes my body did it, but it was unconsious and I didn't choose to do the act." Hence no voluntary bad act.  The other answers are levels of mens rea, or criminal intent under the Model Penal Code.



22.  A.  Powell v. Texas held the statute constitutional.



The other two answers are distractors, but B is the WRONG answer.  Squarely and on the merits opposed to the holding of both Powell v. Texas and Robinson v. California.  These cases are usually discussed to show the prohibition against criminalizing status.  In Powell the court held that unlike Robinson (whose sole crime was being addicted to drugs) the Texas statute punished the act of being in public (while drunk) rather than the status (of being drunk).



23.  State v. Loge was not covered in my law school classes.  I had to go look it up.  However, the answer is A.  The case discussed strict liability, and held that the crime in question was such a strict liability crime.



24.  I need more context for this question.



25.  This question got cut off.  Email me and I will be happy to help.
wal10973@suffolk.edu



6.



Source(s):



2nd Year J.D. student



</answer>

<question>



Translation plagiarism?



I don't actually plan to do this, but out of curiosity I ask: If I were to translate a text or parts of a text (essay, song lyrics, story, etc) from German to English, and use it in my own text, would it (by law) be considered plagiarism?



The thing is: When you translate something, you aren't really copying the original author's work, but paraphrasing it. Consider that it's nearly impossible to translate anything exactly word for word and slight alterations must be made. Therefore, the end translation is in my own words and thus my own work, is it not? What would be the difference if I read what they said and then rewrote it in my own words? (Which is essentially what translating is.) Perhaps I misunderstand something. Please correct me.



For the record, I realize that regardless of the law, copying another's work, without permission or due credit, for my benefit is ethically wrong, but, out of curiosity, this is a question of legality and not of ethics.



</question>



<answer from>



Surak
Member since: 30 March 2008
Total points: 600 (Level 2)



</answer from>

<answer from>



Truth and Justice
Member since: 27 March 2007
Total points: 365 (Level 2)

<answer from>



bcnu
Member since: 04 June 2007
Total points: 3763 (Level 4)



Contributing In:



Law & Ethics



</answer from>



<answer>



Plagiarism means you're misrepresenting earlier work as your own, and a written work contains more than mere words; there are ideas, structure, sequence, etc. The original structure, sequence, characters and such may also be copyrightable, thus a translation would be a derivative work and a copyright infringement in most countries in the world.



On the other hand, if you took a work of non-fiction and rephrased (selected) statements of fact (but not the creative structure or sequence), it would not be a copyright infringement (facts and ideas cannot be copyrighted), but would still be plagiarism if you tried to pass it off without giving credit to the original work.



</answer>

<answer>



You can "legally" move out at any age.  If you runaway and your parents don't go looking for you, it's not against the law.



However, you have to attend school until you're 16.



You're parents are your legal guardians until you're 18.



You can't sign any kind of contract (including renting an apartment) until you are 18.  Can't open a bank account, etc.



So it doesn't do you much good to move out until you're 18.



</answer>

<question>



Fair employment laws definition?



i looking for it and im lost please help me im in school =)



</question>



<answer from>



wizjp
Member since: 05 April 2006
Total points: 193178 (Level 7)



Contributing In:



Law & Ethics



</answer from>



<answer>



Try state department of labor webpage, www.dol.gov, and the eeoc website



</answer>



<answer from>



axhandle...
Member since: 24 March 2006
Total points: 955 (Level 2)



</answer from>



<answer>



You have the right to get and keep a job that you're qualified for — with dignity and without discrimination. Federal, state and local fair employment laws say that no one can deny you a job or treat you differently on the job because of your race, color, age, gender, national origin, ancestry, marital status, religion, sexual orientation, gender identity or disability. These groups are called "protected bases."



Source(s):



http://www.metrokc.gov/dias/ocre/fairemp...



</answer>

ami

Total points: 1751 (Level 3)



</question from>



<question>



Lawsuit question...help please!!?



I fell in a restaurant 2 months ago and hurt my knee pretty badly. I got a lawyer because the insurance company was being difficult to work with. We did the interview with the insurance, sent medical records, etc...



Today I got a call from my lawyer and he said the insurance company had denied the claim. He said the reason was because there wasn't enough witnesses. He told me that this is normal reaction of insurance companies and now we have to file suit.



I have a few questions, though. 1--Is it normal procedure for an insurance company to deny the first claim?



2--If I file suit, how long will the case take?



3--Will it go to court, or will it setlle out of court?



I have about $11,000 medical bills sitting out there that NEED to get paid...I would appreciate all advice!!



Thanks!!!



</question>



<answer from>



MLaw
Member since: 17 July 2006
Total points: 32965 (Level 7)



</answer from>



<answer>



&gt;&gt;  1--Is it normal procedure for an insurance company to deny the first claim?  &lt;&lt;



Yes. Insurance companies make money by collecting premiums; not by paying claims.



&gt;&gt; 2--If I file suit, how long will the case take? &lt;&lt;



As long as necessary to settle or get to trial.  Depending on issues & jurisdiction it could take a couple of months or a couple of years.



&gt;&gt; 3--Will it go to court, or will it setlle out of court? &lt;&lt;



It will settle if you agree to accept what the Ins. company offers to settle it for.



Don't worry about the bills.  Medical providers will agree to accept a lien on the case proceeds for any sums unpaid by your insurance.



</answer>



<answer from>



zippo
Member since: 20 April 2006
Total points: 3497 (Level 4)



</answer from>



<answer>



An insurance company is going to look after their interests, which does not include paying you.  They want to resolve it as cheaply as they can.  They may still settle, but only if the odds of them losing a court case compel them to do so.  The time it could take to settle a suit could take quite a while, maybe a year or more.  Interviews will need to be conducted with any witnesses, then you will have to go through a deposition, an informal hearing with the concerning attorneys, in which you will be questioned on the details of your accident.  They will follow this procedure for all witnesses as well, and then assess their position.  They may at that point decide to settle, or proceed to trial, depending on the strength of your case.



I would suggest that you contact the doctors office about your bills and let them know that your bill with them is headed for litigation, and that you will settle your bill with them promptly once your case is resolved.  Otherwise, they will refer the matter to collections, which can have an adverse effect on your credit rating.
Good Luck.



</answer>



<answer from>



candy g
Member since: 07 September 2006
Total points: 16479 (Level 6)



</answer from>



<answer>



see if they will agree to mediation and in our area the current wait to get into court for this type of issue is over a year.



Get your lawyer to have the bills as medical liens that way it does not hurt you as much on your credit report.



We got our claim put in writing by the duty manager on the night of our daughters injury on their headed paper, makes it a bit harder to wiggle out of it at a later date.........



</answer>

Politics & Government

<question from>



chariank...
Member since: 14 April 2006
Total points: 117 (Level 1)



</question from>



<question>



What's the difference between av, bv and cv rated lawyers?



</question>



<answer from>



jadedwom...
Member since: 21 October 2008
Total points: 928 (Level 2)



</answer from>



<answer>



Its a kind of peer rated review based on what other attorneys think of your skills as a lawyer.  AV is the highest rating and CV is the "lowest" (but its not a bad rating, its simply the lowest measure they use).  And there are plenty of attorneys who aren't rated at all - it can be a big popularity contest.



</answer>



<answer from>



flazatty
Member since: 21 April 2006
Total points: 2458 (Level 3)



</answer from>



<answer>



av bv and cv are rankings developed by Martindale-Hubbell, the publisher of a legal directory that lists most lawyers in the United States.  The ratings are developed based on comments received from other lawyers in confidential surveys.  Not all lawyers have a rating.  The fact that a lawyer is unrated does not necessarily mean there is anything wrong.  Some lawyers request that they not be rated.



av is the highest, bv is in the middle, and cv is the lowest rating.



You should go with a lawyer who is av rated.  bv is okay.  I would stay away from the cv rated lawyer, however, because that indicates that other lawyers have questions about his or her legal ability.



</answer>

<question>



Can you make a contract with a minor? who will win?



Lilac A. Rugg, an adult, entered into a contract to sell an automobile for $4,750.00 to Hope Ferterbest, a minor.  Delivery was set for the next day.  Rugg learned that Hope was a minor before she came to pick up the car.  When Hope arrived, Lilac refused to accept the money and refused to deliver the car saying that she did not want to deal with a minor.  Hope sued to enforce the contract.
Will Hope win?  Why or why not?



</question>



<answer from>



jolyth_a
Member since: 25 January 2008
Total points: 6904 (Level 5)



</answer from>



<answer>



Hope is a minor and as such is not eligible to enter into a legal contract. She needs the approval of her parents or guardian to enter into a contract and neither party can be held liable.



Do your own homework



</answer>



<answer from>



Mark
Member since: 20 March 2007
Total points: 21989 (Level 6)



Contributing In:



Law & Ethics



</answer from>



<answer>



Lilac will win.  A minor is not permitted under law to contract in their own name. If Lilac would accept payment and deliver the automobile, Hope could some time later ask for her money back because it was not a valid contract of sale.  Yes, the courts would force Lilac to give the money back.



A contract for necessities of life (e.g., food) would be enforceable.



</answer>



<answer from>



sgoldper...
Member since: 18 May 2006
Total points: 56488 (Level 7)



</answer from>



<answer>

</answer>



<answer from>



Sweethar...
Member since: 17 December 2005
Total points: 20502 (Level 6)



</answer from>



<answer>



Lilac will win because a contract for the sale of an automobile is not enforceable with a minor.

davidmi7...
Member since: 05 January 2006
Total points: 59853 (Level 7)



Contributing In:



Law & Ethics



</answer from>



<answer>



The short answer: pack and move



Now, a little more detail.
If you are month to month, the landlord can give you 30 days notice to vacate. This is not technically an eviction, this is simply the end of the business relationship as allowed by the contract and/or state law. The landlord does not need a reason to do this.



If you are still on a fixed period lease and it has not expired, then he would need to get a court ordered eviction. At the hearing you can present evidence that you are in compliance with the lease and he will present evidence that you are not.



Many leases limit the amount of time you can have guests that are not on the lease stay in the apartment. You can be evicted for violation of that as well.



If you are on a fixed period lease, you can assume that even if you stop the eviction your landlord will not allow you to remain after the lease expires and switched to month to month.



There seems to be some underlying sentiment that landlords want to "screw" with people. Simply not the case. They want the rent paid and no damage done to the units. Empty apartments are lost income. Getting new tenants is expensive. They would rather keep a good tenant for many years than to look for a new one every 6 months.



</answer>



<answer from>



M S
Member since: 18 October 2006
Total points: 5297 (Level 5)



</answer from>



<answer>



Simple: forget apartments.



Seriously I've lived at the 2 highest rated complexes in a major city and they both had a habit of trying to accuse me if things like "breaking their a/c system".  And that's from a guy with a full time job, a near-perfect credit rating, and no late payments whatsoever.



You can probably fight them legally.  But, by the time you get a verdict, even if you win, the legal costs will most likely near bankrupt you.  Probably not worth the effort...



If you need a quick place to move, stay at a by-the-week motel and put the rest of your goods in storage while you search for property to own.
----------------------



Once you've found a good (usually $45000-$60000 in a safe suburban area)  condo, get together $10000 or so and make a 30% down payment on it (simple fixed-rate mortgage, avoid dicey adjustable and balloon mortgages at all costs).



Your monthly payment + association fee for a one-bedroom should be $600 or less...and, since you own it, you can have, for example, a boyfriend staying there and not have to worry about any "double occupancy charges" BS.



Best luck...



</answer>

<question from>



bootlegt...
Member since: 09 February 2006
Total points: 258 (Level 2)



</question from>



<question>



Can anyone please help with this question about Business Law?



QUESTION:  Josh and Brett enter a contract in which Brett agrees to write a how-to guide for Josh’s publishing company for $500. Brett gets into the project, and calls Josh to tell him that the project is entirely too much work for $500, and that he won’t be performing his obligations under the contract. Josh decides to wait to see if Brett will change his mind. Brett does change his mind before the time his performance is required, and calls Josh to tell him he’ll be meeting his contractual obligations. Josh tells Brett to forget it; he won’t pay him the $500. The most likely result will be that



ANSWER CHOICES:



A. Brett may successfully sue Josh for breach of contract if Josh fails to pay him after Brett performs his obligations because Brett retracted his repudiation.



B. Brett will lose if he sues Josh for breach of contract because Brett repudiated the contract.



C. Josh may successfully sue Brett for anticipatory repudiation, even though he waited to allow Josh the opportunity to perform his obligations.



THANK YOU.



</question>



<answer from>



Lea
Member since: 20 December 2007
Total points: 219 (Level 1)



</answer from>



<answer>



I say A



</answer>



<answer from>



Azuriel8...
Member since: 01 October 2008
Total points: 127 (Level 1)



</answer from>



<answer>



B.  Brett intended to repudiate, and communicated this fact to Josh.  At this point, Josh is under no obligation to act immediately, and can sue for breach.  Although having waited, a new contract needs to be entered into.  Brett's later change of mind constitutes a new offer, but without Josh's agreement at that point of time, there can be no contract.  As such Brett will lose if he tries to sue.



</answer>



<answer from>



robrobii...
Member since: 24 July 2008
Total points: 7591 (Level 5)



Contributing In:



Economics



Singles & Dating



</answer from>



<answer>



A



</answer>



<answer from>



bcnu
Member since: 04 June 2007
Total points: 3763 (Level 4)



Contributing In:



Law & Ethics



</answer from>



<answer>



(B). Once the contract was clearly repudiated, the contract was over, Josh had the right to contract another writer to perform all of Brett's tasks, and it would be unfair for Brett to expect to be paid after Josh had no further expectation that the work would be performed by Brett, even if no alternative writer had been paid. Answer (C) would not be true unless Josh changed his position (spent the money somewhere else) after Brett repudiated, thus causing damages.



However, Brett and Josh could certainly form a NEW agreement, or simply ratify and re-institute the old one by mutual assent, thus giving each the benefit of the original bargain.



</answer>

Warren D
Member since: 16 June 2006

</answer from>



<answer>



It could depend on the zoning ordinances in your city.  Most cities don't like to have derelict vehicles parked anywhere in sight.  Yours probably isn't in that category, but the police won't really know that.



Your best bet would be to either keep the vehicle out of sight--as you are doing now--or get it registered.



Many cities have ordinances prohiting parking recreational vehicles in driveways.  This is probably a bit more common than most people think.  Also, they may object to commercial vehicles--such as driver-owned big rigs--being parked in neighborhoods.



If it were me I'd go take a look at that city ordinance.



</answer>

<answer from>



Simon M
Member since: 02 September 2008
Total points: 611 (Level 2)



</answer from>



<answer>



Unfortunately its too late to report it to the police as it has to be reported in six months.  your insurer should have obtained witness details, and as long as he/she is independent you can use it in court.  Your insurer still can report it to the third party insurer who then has three months to investigate the claim, at the end they have to either admit liability or give their reasons for disputing.  Third party insurers can admit liability on a without prejudice basis, meaning they will deal with the claim and pay you out but not officially admit liability.  Go back to you insurer and demand they make a third party first report, and report it themselves.  The insurer is fobbing you off.
The statute of limitations for making a non injury claim is 6 years, and 3 for injury.



Source(s):



Ex Solicitor and now a Police Officer



</answer>

<answer>



Hey there, first and foremost has these allegations had a direct influence on your job, your child's environment, school, etc?  If it has had  some sort of influence you can absolutely take legal action.



Secondly, allegations like these need to have evidentiary support to make them valid in any court of law.  This would be a civil matter of good old fashioned feuding neighbors and I actually went through something like this when I was a teenager, and believe me it was no fun.



Has this person put these allegations in writing?  Does this family live close to you?



Have you tried resolving this on your own?



One ace that you have in the hole is that you know that the father in the family is a cop.  What is being done to you could be considered profiling whether it's racial, religious or whatever you want to classify it as.... Police officers can get fired for profiling you see that stuff on the news all the time... as an officer of the law they are supposed to treat everyone the same....



I suggest going straight to his superior and let that person know what is going on... does this help?  Is there anymore info to this story that might help with providing an answer?



I hope this helps,
xoxo



Jacqueline



</answer>



<answer from>



zippo
Member since: 20 April 2006
Total points: 3493 (Level 4)



</answer from>



<answer>



You best chance would be to get sworn depositions from the parties that heard any libelous statements, they would have to hear it directly from the family you spoke of or it is considered hearsay, and would not be admissible as evidence in a court proceeding.  Unless you are being harassed by them either by phone, mailings, or face to face confrontations, a harassment suit wouldn't have too much merit either I'm afraid.  Libel is not not a criminal matter, so you would need to obtain a attorney that specializes in civil matters.  Usually you need to prove that you were damaged in some way over the libelous statements.



Good Luck.



</answer>

Prisons serve several functions:



1) Rehabilitation.
2) Punishment for crimes.
3) Protection for non-criminals.



Many people argue against number 1. But even if they are completely correct, number 2 is important, and number 3 is critical.

<answer from>



Gravy
Member since: 16 April 2008
Total points: 13989 (Level 6)



Contributing In:



Small Business



Dental



Law & Ethics



</answer from>



<answer>



It's illegal for the McCain campaign to spend money that way.



It isn't illegal for the RNC, though, and they're the ones who paid the bills.



Source(s):



http://news.yahoo.com/s/ap/20081022/ap_o...



</answer>



<answer from>



tonalc2
Member since: 20 June 2008
Total points: 22104 (Level 6)



Contributing In:



San Francisco



Elections



</answer from>



<answer>



According to an election law (sponsored by McCain), clothing cannot be purchased with campaign donations if it could be considered for personal use:



"...used to fulfill any commitment, obligation, or expense of a person that would exist irrespective of the candidate's election campaign or individual's duties as a holder of Federal office, including--



`(A) a home mortgage, rent, or utility payment;



`(B) a clothing purchase;



`(C) a noncampaign-related automobile expense..."



However, I believe the money comes from the RNC, not the campaign directly, so this would not be covered.



</answer>

<answer>



<evaluation>Best Answer - Chosen by Asker</evaluation>



If it isn't on the endangered species list and it was humanely killed then the only thing the person has done wrong is parade his offensive yobbishness on facebook.



If he went to the local park and snared it or bashed it with a blunt instrument, kicked it to death or the like then those things would be illegal, causing unnecessary suffering.

<question>



Was religion the first form of law?



</question>



<answer from>



Malruhn
Member since: 27 April 2008
Total points: 3947 (Level 4)



</answer from>



<answer>



<evaluation>Best Answer - Chosen by Asker</evaluation>



I believe that rather than religion, the first form of law was social contract, like Laughter was saying.



A bully that beats people up all the time is detrimental to the survival of a group - so a group consensus that beatings are bad would have been the first form of formalized rules.  Same goes for the person in charge gets the best cut of food when dinner-time comes... it's all social agreement.



Religion came along later.  So instead of US saying that beatings are bad, it's the Divine Order saying that beatings are bad.



Source(s):



Sociology are my life!



</answer>

There is no requirement that you see this child, whether yours or not.  Thats first.  On a more fundemental level, you do have a duty to pay child support for a child that is shown to be your legal offspring. If the mother should get with someone who wants to adopt the child, then you have the option of signing away your parental rights in return for a release of liability on the support claim.



One word of advice: if you come off in a courtroom as the heartless uncaring jerk that you did here in this forum, I wouldnt count on a judge being to sympathetic to you.  After all, the child didnt ask to be born into this melodrama. <question from>



Gerry K
Member since: 08 May 2008
Total points: 99 (Level 1)



</question from>



<question>



How do I file for a copyright?



for song lyrics



</question>



<answer from>



kbmisfit
Member since: 24 April 2007
Total points: 131 (Level 1)



</answer from>



<answer>



You need to prove that you created that song on a certain date.  And on that date the music was automatically copyrighted. Put your music or song in some tangible form - a piece of paper, a CD, a floppy disk or written as sheet music or even on a napkin. Just remember - it HAS to be in a tangible form. Also include a copyright notice at the end of your work. The proper format is: Copyright or ©, year you wrote it, your name, ex: © 2000 John Doe or Copyright 2000 John Doe.



Then you have to find a copyright office, Google it with your state and city, and they will give you a process to go through.



</answer>



<answer from>



bcnu
Member since: 04 June 2007
Total points: 3763 (Level 4)



Contributing In:



Law & Ethics



</answer from>



<answer>



Assuming you have a reason to file (copyright is automatic), such as wanting statutory right to damages, or to sue an infringer, you simply logon to the copyright.gov page, download the proper forms, fill them out, and send them in with the necessary (minimal) fee and the required copies of the lyrics.



Source(s):



http://www.copyright.gov



</answer>

<question>



What is the North Dakota penalty for marijuana paraphernalia possession?



Someone I know was just arrested for having a marijuana pipe in his carry-on bag at the airport. He has never been arrested before and has no criminal record. I'm trying to find out what the consequences are likely to be. There were no drugs and no other contraband or crimes committed, just the pipe itself. He's intending to plead guilty, apologize, and say he just wants to go home (if that has any relevance to likely sentencing.) Do you think they'll take it to trial? If they don't, what's likely to happen? Will they just set him free? Will he be in jail overnight or will he be processed today? (He was arrested around 9 AM or so.)



I've been able to determine that possession of marijuana paraphernalia alone is a class A misdemeanor, but I can't find any information about what the sentencing guidelines for this particular crime are. Thank you in advance for any information you can provide.



Or, since it was at the airport, is it even going to be considered a North Dakota crime or will he be taken under Federal law? I don't know and I need to find out. Again, thank you in advance.



</question>



</answer from>



<answer>



Class A misdemeanor, for which a maximum penalty of one year's imprisonment, a fine of two thousand dollars, or both, may be imposed.



That is if you correctly found that this crime is a class A misdemeanor.



Keep in mind these are MAXIMUM sentences.  This is often left at the discretion of the judge whether or not he simply fines "your friend" or even sentences him to any jail time period.



Source(s):



http://www.legis.nd.gov/cencode/t121c32....



</answer>

<question from>



buitoni
Member since: 19 June 2008
Total points: 100 (Level 1)



</question from>



<question>



Rights of residency???????????



need halp/advice/tips ,,i live in a house that is in my partners name i.e her name is on mortgage etc etc ,,i have always contributed toward bills ,houshold expenses etc & this has been my residence for some time ,,she has now meet someone else & wants me to vacate the premises ,,what are my rights ,what should i do or act to do????



</question>



<answer from>



Kit Fang
Member since: 12 February 2007
Total points: 7118 (Level 5)



</answer from>



<answer>



Go talk to a lawyer, because cases like this are always sort of complicated.



Often if you were to take her to court they would want to know how much you actually paid, etc. so keep a record of any bills you have paid etc.



</answer>



<answer from>



foo_figh...
Member since: 12 June 2006
Total points: 2215 (Level 3)



</answer from>



<answer>



Unfortunately the law as it stands at the moment wasn't written with your kind of situation in mind, however the law commission has made proposals to change that. There are however a patchwork of legal tools that can be used in your situation. Firstly I would need to know if you made a contribution at the time of the propertys purchase whether it be to the deposit, or right to buy or assumption of responsibility on the mortgage (which clearly you dont have). If you have one of those you have whats known as a direct contribution which will make establishing a trust over the property easier. In trust law an express common intention constructive trust (ECICT) may assist you. You need evidence of an express understanding, agreement or arrangement that the property is to belong to both of you i.e. did she at any time say anything to lead you to believe that the property was as much yours as hers? If so there must be a detrimental reliance on that understanding. This reliance must be something which cant be attributed to mere love and affection, so for example redoing the kitchen or some other form of home improvement.



If there is no express common intention, there may be an inferred common intention constructive trust (ICICT). With an ICICT a common intention can be inferred by direct or indirect contributions. Direct contributions are explained above and require the contribution to be made at the time of purchasing the property. However it would appear to me that you have made indirect contributions which are generally any other contribution to the cost of living. As one judge put it in Le Foe v Le Foe, it is an arbitrary measure to draw a distinction between direct contributions and other contributions which free up the partners wages to be used on the mortgage. So your contributions to the mortgage even though you havent assumed any liability under it should provide you with a share in the property, the size of which will depend on how much you contributed. Do not misinterpret though, this would give you an equitable interest in the property i.e. it is held on trust for you, not an interest in the property itself.



</answer>

<question from>



Really Now?
Member since: 10 August 2008
Total points: 162 (Level 1)



</question from>



<question>



What is the fundamental and/or legal difference between a "marriage" and an "union"?



What factor or factors make them different from each other? Do you think they are just the same, but different words?



</question>



<additional details>



Well if a marriage involves a religious ceremony then why is the government involved in how a marriage should be defined?



</additional details>



<answer from>



glenhand...
Member since: 22 June 2006
Total points: 2247 (Level 3)



</answer from>



<answer>



Marriage is a contract taken between to adults, before God and friends. You enter the contract with a pledge to love, honor and obey. This works both ways. A union has no contract and no Honor given to God. This is how male animals act.



</answer>



<answer from>



halltris...
Member since: 13 March 2006
Total points: 240 (Level 1)



</answer from>



<answer>



Marriage and Union are technically the same within common law. For example, two people living together for 7 years are therefore considered legally married. The time until this "legal marriage" is considered union, or cohabitation - depending on where you live, the term is different. It is only common belief that marriage is legally different than union, as while there is some difference, the law approaches both the same. A great example of this would deal with domestic violence. In a case of domestic violence, one person could be given a protection order against the other, however, a protection order is only possible under law if either the two people are married, lived together, or were family. So technically speaking, and to hopefully make sense of this, your relationship with your mother, in law, is considered a union, and obviously you shouldn't be married. The two terms are basically used to determine the status of two individual people, even though the two terms are used in conjunction with one another.



</answer>



<answer from>



Jason
Member since: 03 May 2008
Total points: 551 (Level 2)



</answer from>



<answer>



Simply put, a marriage is something that involves a religious ceremony, and is a religious right. This is performed by a pastor, preacher, minister or priest, etc.



A union is a civil ceremony, and does not contain the religious portions of the marriage ceremony. This would be performed by a mayor, judge, magistrate, ship captain (in international waters), etc.



In the end, the result is the same.



</answer>

<answer>



Why don't we join the rest of the western civilized world and abolish capital punishment.



It only would work if murderers were capable of rational thought instead of being motivated by alcohol, drugs and testosterone.



Do away with it entirely and spend the savings in ways that prevent murders. Most of the expense is up front at the investigation and trial stage and serves only to let grandstanding prosecutors pander to the public by showing how tough they are.



The death penalty isn't even a blip on the criminal radar screen and hasn't been significant for over a century. During the last 100 years, the most people executed in one year was 198 in 1935. For a nation of 300 million people that is statistically zero and the 50 or so in 2007 is statistically 25% of zero.



The expense of keeping capital punishment is enormous. For example, the state of Maryland figures it has spent $37 Million EACH for the executions it has carried out since reinstating the death penalty. When you look at the costs, this is a criminal waste of the public's money.



</answer>

<answer from>



Laredo
Member since: 25 April 2006
Total points: 8836 (Level 5)



</answer from>



<answer>



The free online forms DO NOT meet the requirements of any state.  You can purchase Divorce Forms online from http://www.uslegal.com for a very small fee and print them off.  They are made by a group of lawyers and are drawn up according to the laws of each state.



We bought the estate forms package from US Legal and they were accepted by the Court in our state to Probate a Will.  The complete packet (about a dozen forms) cost us $39.95.  If we had used an attorney it would have cost us $2,500.00.  The divorce forms probably won't cost you as much as ours cost us.



A note of caution, your must TYPE in the information rather than print it by hand or the courts may reject it.



I hope this information helps.



</answer>

<question from>



- -
Member since: 15 October 2008
Total points: 91 (Level 1)



</question from>



<question>



Is torrent piracy really a crime (without trying to deny that legally it is) ?



Like I said, I'm not denying it being breaking the law with big trouble for you if you spread copyrighted stuff via BitTorrent for instance, but in your opinion how much harm is it?



Let's put it this way - I once knocked onto a forum, where a certain program was discussed and somebody asked for help about it while somehow mentioning that he had torrented it. One of the honest buyers immediately stormed him with "...torrenting it is just like stealing it off a store shelf." Now everybody ought to understand that even if you did pirate a program , NEVER mention it on a forum ;), but I should point out that it isn't exactly like stealing it off a store shelf in the sense that all he got was an exact copy of an online store item which many others have downloaded after paying and no box material or transportation fees ever went into that exact copy specifically.
This is where I'll point out that of course somebody needs to get payed for compiling the program (which is far from an easy task) and if everybody just pirated it , the maker would just go bankrupt. But, if you think about it, isn't buying a non-material form of information, like a movie or something , online simply getting it free and donating the price to the makers to fund their continuous work? What if even without it being available as a torrent, I wouldn't buy it no matter how I wanted it and simply got it this way because it was free? It'd mean that I wouldn't have payed anyway, but didn't cause any material damage by getting it this way either. You heard of this Deviance thing going on around torrenting sites? It's this group of computer users who release free torrents of games while always adding this message to a readme file that comes along - "After playing it, buy it. We did." And that's the golden truth - if you want the makers to make a good sequal, some other good product or improve the existing , go ahead and pay them! They need it to continue work, bring the bread on the table and pay their bills ;). But if you just get a copy like in the instance afore mentioned, is it bad? Better yet, what if the program has an active online community going about it and by getting it this way you simply start actively taking part in it, contributing to it aso. while not having enough money to buy it in any imaginable closer time? I mean software isn't food - you don't NEED it, so pirating it can't be called disrupting the economy of highly necessary goods. In fact making it is just taking the risk of no-one bothering to buy it anyway, unless of course you're fulfilling the people's hidden desires and cutting a buck of of it ;), which of course isn't part of the overall economy's backbone. So, yeah.
If you read all the mumbo-jumbo above, you probably understood that I'm not trying to initiate some crazy revolution against copyright but just squeezing out your opinions ;). So, who's first?



</question>



<additional details>



By the way, I'll note that in a broad sense I only meant entertainment based stuff. I just didn't want to put the word "game" everywhere so's you'd think I'm a big Quake junkie X).



</additional details>



<answer from>



Da Komrade!
Member since: 01 January 2008
Total points: 12915 (Level 6)



Contributing In:



Elections



</answer from>



<answer>



My opinion is that if you have to go through all of this rationalizing to justify an action in your mind, then it's obviously wrong.  The product isn't yours, the people who produced it put time and effort into it for the purpose of selling it, and it is their property.  If you take it without paying for it, it's theft.



</answer>



<answer from>



Stephen Arabacus Pulp
Member since: 07 August 2008
Total points: 1515 (Level 3)



</answer from>



<answer>



Obviously it's a crime in most western countries, so I assume we're talking about ethics here.



You approached it in almost a Kantian way, I'd look at it from more of a Marxist perspective. The question is, does the creator have a right to the exclusive ownership thing that she created? If she does, then yes it is unethical for you to download a torrent, because that infringes on her right to share it with you on her terms. You'd have to read some Marx to decide what you think about that. I won't get into it, suffice to say I'm not so sure she does have that right.



I'm in two minds about this. On the one hand, the Marxist in me thinks it promotes a sense of communal property and free exchange of ideas, and so I think piracy's good. On the other hand the polite me feels a bit wrong for simply taking from the creator who does not share my views. A decent enough argument can be made for why the creator of the film/music/game has the right to distribute it as she pleases, who am i to simply decide she is wrong without debate?



I tend to decide though that the polite me just has a hard time comprehending sophisticated philosophical argument, just like the ironically named gentleman above.



</answer>

<answer>

If you're in the UK, there are centres set up especially for parents to drop off their kids so the non-custodial parent can see their child under supervision, but they can still do normal parent things, it's like a supervised day care centre.

<question from>



LifeHapp...
Member since: 31 May 2007
Total points: 6671 (Level 5)



</question from>



<question>



Does anyone know the "technicality"?



that allowed William Ayers to escape prosecution for his involvement in the bombings?.Since he has admitted he was involved there has to be some reason he was not prosecuted..



thanks for any insight.



</question>



<answer from>



Teekno
Member since: 26 November 2006
Total points: 17159 (Level 6)



Contributing In:



Law & Ethics



Elections



</answer from>



<answer>



He was charged, but those charges were later dropped. The government said it was for "national security" reasons, but the generally accepted theory is that the evidence against him was inadmissible.



In the late 60s and early 70s, the FBI did a lot of bugging and wiretapping without benefit of a warrant. At the time, judges were sympathetic to the government and would admit these as evidence. That changed in the early 70s, and government prosecutors often found themselves with cases that were completely built upon non-admissible evidence. That's most likely what happened with the Ayers case.



</answer>



<answer from>



The Hammer
Member since: 17 August 2007
Total points: 13179 (Level 6)



Contributing In:



Elections



</answer from>



<answer>



Illegal wire taps.



</answer>



<answer from>



Pete
Member since: 21 June 2006
Total points: 10656 (Level 6)



Contributing In:



Programming & Design



</answer from>



<answer>



It wasn't a technicality. That does not even come close to giving it justice.



First, let's put this out there: Ayers is a lunatic, and does not deserve the freedom he has. The irony is that the reason he has freedom is because the government he protested against actually worked the way he demanded it should.



What people are calling a technicality is known amongst legal scholars as COINTELPRO. It was a practice of illegal actions by the FBI that make the shady nature of the patriot act look transparent and noble. These actions were akin to the modern raping of the Constitution by men who were drunk on power. They violated the constitution on a daily basis, and they attempted to usurp many of the freedoms we have fought and continue to fight for in the world.



Nearly all of the evidence against Ayers was obtained through this practice, and as such it was tossed. The government corrected itself, and in the process, had to let a terrorist go free.



It is actually a thing of beauty, that our country is so dedicated to our ideals that we will let scum like him go. I know, it makes ya sick that he walks free...but man, this is one helluva place to live when we are so dedicated to our freedom that we won't give it up for anything.



People who call this a "technicality" are simply not informed about the facts. There wasn't a wrong address on a warrant. This was using the Constitution as toilet paper for nearly a decade.



</answer>

<answer>



<evaluation>Best Answer - Chosen by Asker</evaluation>



&gt;&gt; what percentage of prisoners have been exonerated since DNA became a tool &lt;&lt;



Such a question cannot be answered. Percentage requires a fixed population & a fixed definition of "exonerated."



If you want to know absolute numbers you can Google the "Innocence Project."



But they may have a vested interest in definitions that would be most expansive.



</answer>

serrerra

<answer>



To my knowledge (as I have rented under similar circumstances), the property owner has to provide you with another place to live and give you 30 days notice if she is going to fix the problem. And keep in mind, though she is threatening you with eviction often, the fact that you have a child guarantees you a 30 day notice (whether your lease says it or not). Landlords are supposed to be able to maintain your properties, and if it gets as far as court, that's the way the judge will see it, too. Stand your ground and if it gets too far, you always have a right to a trial. Best of luck and congrats on the baby! (6 months late. :)  )



</answer>



<answer from>



Mia B
Member since: 19 September 2008
Total points: 125 (Level 1)



</answer from>



<answer>



Each state has different landlord/tenant laws.  Your best bet would be to contact your local legal help office.  They will help you find someone who can answer your question properly. Check your phone book, internet or you can look at the legal ads in your local newspaper normally at the end of the ad they write the legal aide's office phone number.  You can also contact the Your County or State's Bar Association.



</answer>

<question>



Could this be parental kidnapping? What should we do?



So monday my kid brother went of to school. My 16 year old brother, and our mother went to pick him up. But they said my father signed him out already at 2:30pm. We've learned a day later, he was taken off to GA. Another day later enrolled into a new Middle School. My brother has already expressed he doesn't want to be in GA. He'd rather be with us, mom, myself, and our brother & his friends here in FL.



Mom and I aren't sure how to go about this issue. Due to the fact my father has challenged her mental state. She isn't sure if she goes up there to get him herself or even if he learns that my mom is in the state, he my try to have her committed there or Backer ACT or something.

soupasta...
Member since: 02 January 2008

<answer>



<evaluation>Best Answer - Chosen by Asker</evaluation>



because they are not divorced and because neither has sole custody with any sort of legal binding ruling of visitation or whatever, it IS NOT KIDNAPPING.



i would question that anxiety disorder would hardly justify involuntary committment.



they both have equal custody and as of now this is a domestic dispute. from your description, no crime has occurred.



it is not a crime for a father to take a child out of state without telling his wife.



no judgement has been entered regarding her mental state and your description did not even mention evaluation by a competent mental health professional.



just because he says she's crazy doesn't make it true. its called psychological abuse.



now if the father or mother just disappeared with the child and the other spouse had no way of knowing at all where the child or other spouse had gone... missing persons, yes....



if the mom runs with the child, it will further substantiate the fathers claims of psychological illness to the point of endangering the child. if the mother and child run without the father knowing where they went or when they would come back, i can't think of too many psychologists that would testify that she was competent to care for a minor, and the act of suddenly disappearing with the child could be grounds to demonstrate psychosis- paranoid delusions, delusions of persecution, and blatant disregard for the best interest of the child.



Source(s):



US Code



</answer>

<answer>



If this is a U.S. question: You don't have to take a drug test unless it is court ordered or you are on parole or probation. You never have to take a lie detector test, although if you are applying for certain jobs, i.e. in law enforcement, you will be required to take the test to be considered for the job.

jellybea...

<answer>



No.



Those are two different tests with a variety of purposes. Some job applications may require both. But there are other situations where you would only take one. Probation officers give drug tests, for instance. And police interrogators may use lie detector tests on suspects.

<question>



With over two million people in us prisons do you think the criminal and justice system is working?



</question>



<answer from>



Phate
Member since: 14 July 2008
Total points: 2403 (Level 3)



</answer from>



<answer>



<evaluation>Best Answer - Chosen by Asker</evaluation>



Obviously not, prisons are getting more crowded every month.



The problem isn't that we don't have enough prisons, its that once inmates go into the outside world they have no job or future, and don't anything other than to be in a gang or deal drugs to survive.



Justice system should focus more on rehabilitating prisoners so they have a job once they get out, instead of keeping them locked up.



</answer>



<asker's comment>



im in uk and prison doesn't work thanks for the answer



</asker's comment>



<answer from>



Honestly, now
Member since: 31 December 2006
Total points: 4010 (Level 4)



</answer from>



<answer>



This begs the question: What do you want the criminal justice system to do?  If the answer is "reduce crime", then no it's not working at all.  Prison dooms people to a life of crime.  I just moved out of an area with a ton of revolving-door criminals and know from firsthand experience that the conditioning you get in prison, the rapes, beatings, and willingness to kill over any disrespect is totally incompatible with life in a civilized society.  Or america.  If, on the other hand, you presume the purpose of the criminal justice system to be to keep the population cowed and afraid of the government, then it's working just fine.



If you view the CJ system as broken and want to fix it, there's an obvious answer.  Legalize drugs.  There's no need to turn a pothead into a violent person, but that's what prison does to a million americans a year.  I'll get up on my soapbox for a bit here.  Think about this--instead of all the money we pour into cops, courts, and prisons, we could spend a tenth of that maintaining crackhouses.  I don't just think crack should be legal, I think it should be free.  It's a self-correcting problem.  Just wheel in a cart of crack at night and drag out the bodies in the morning.  It's not real compassionate, but neither is the current system.  This would be a lot cheaper and you'd have the same end result.



</answer>

Mohsan S

<answer>



If you found some damage that wasn't your fault then you should've reported it. But if you haven't then they might think it was your fault. I don't understand why you didn't pay the last month rent knowing that it would cost you the earth to update. Go see a solicitor they are expensive but are very helpful.

<answer from>



Bardic
Member since: 21 November 2006
Total points: 44050 (Level 7)



</answer from>



<answer>



That's what a guarantee is for, I'm afraid. If you don't pay your rent they are entitled to call upon your guarantor.



The best you can do is pay up now, before they continue with enforcing the guarantee.



Regarding the damages, if you didn't report them at the outset of course they'll hold you responsible. In this respect, you need to take advice, either from the CAB or a solicitor.



</answer>

<question from>



Yes! An extender!
Member since: 07 March 2008
Total points: 2693 (Level 4)



</question from>



<question>



Different types of courts and cases?



I need to watch a couple of cases in court but my guidelines are from an Australian university so obviously things are a little different here.



First I had to see a summary court hearing which I did in a local magistrates court.  This was the defendant's first time there and he pleaded guilty (they asked right at the beginning how he pleaded).  I was told that I went to a sentence case not a summary hearing.  Confused on that...can anyone advise?



Now I need to view a criminal jury trial at a higher court.  Isn't this likely to be really long and over a few days?  What would be this court in either West Sussex or Surrey (if possible).



Also a higher court plea of guilty and sentence hearing.



Any words of wisdom greatly appreciated.  Eg. where to go, what in UK terms I should be looking for and how long its likely to be.  Thank you!



</question>



<answer from>



lovs2jok...
Member since: 02 September 2008
Total points: 5865 (Level 5)



</answer from>



<answer>



The four basic types of court cases are civil, criminal, juvenile, and traffic.



A civil case is a disagreement between two or more people or businesses. Examples of a civil case include disputes between a landlord and tenant, divorce actions, small claims cases, and a case where one person is suing another for damages.



A criminal case involves a person who has been accused of committing either a misdemeanor or a felony offense.



Generally, a juvenile case usually involves a minor who is under the age of seventeen. Juvenile cases are heard by the family division of the circuit court. The three types of juvenile cases are juvenile delinquency cases, child protective hearings and traffic cases.



Finally, the most common type of court case is related to a traffic violation. A traffic violation may be either a civil infraction or a misdemeanor.



</answer>



<answer from>



Bardic
Member since: 21 November 2006
Total points: 44058 (Level 7)



</answer from>



<answer>



Just the luck of the draw, really. Defendants are always asked how they plead right at the start of any court case; if they plead guilty the court will proceed, having heard any evidence about the seriousness of the case, directly to sentencing. Sometimes, in a Crown Court case, the judge will adjourn to receive reports on the defendant before sentencing.



The system is that summary cases are heard in the magistrates court, unless the magistrates consider that it is too serious or the sentence that might be given is beyond their powers.  In that case, they will send it to the Crown Court, which is the "next one up".  Sometimes the defendant can opt to go directly to Crown Court.



Crown Court trials are heard before judge and jury (unless there's a guilty plea)



I'd suggest you try for another magistrate's case and see if there's a not guilty plea.  In that case, you'll see the whole thing in operation.



Regarding the Crown Court, speak to the court staff and explain what you need.  They're invariably helpful, though they'll not be able to say with any accuracy how long the case might go on.



</answer>

<question>



My friend just died. The Family vultures have swooped in. What recourse does his loved one have?

<answer from>



LovesThe...
Member since: 20 July 2008
Total points: 5706 (Level 5)



Contributing In:



Law & Ethics



</answer from>



<answer>



<evaluation>Best Answer - Chosen by Asker</evaluation>



Her attorney can explain all her options to her.



If persons, even siblings, take was is not theirs, that is called theft.  Until the matter can be handled through probate, the deceased property remains in his estate.  The executor or administrator of the estate will have a responsibility to recover property that has been taken and otherwise to protect the assets and value of the estate.



Even if there is no will, the surviving spouse or partner or other person may apply to the probate court for authorization as executor or administrator.  That person will have consulted an attorney prior to taking action.



Source(s):



years of experience in probate court



</answer>

gotwheel...

<answer>



A fact of law: if you do not have a will, even if you do and it is not notarized it is not valid. The procedure for distributing an estate vary but are generally the same. It starts with the closet relative, usually the wife, then kids of the deceased, siblings and so on.Just because the person has a boyfriend or girlfriend doesn't mean anything. Unless they were common law. Even with a will and an executor, that doesn't mean everything is going to be easy. The executor has control and can dispose of the estate as he sees fit unless it is spelled out. For example: if a free and clear car is involved, unless it is spelled out on who gets it, the executor can dispose of it for a price he determines. He is under no law , rule or obligation to sell it for market value or a second offer. All he is bound to, is to deposit the proceeds in the estate account. You as beneficiaries have every right to contest a will. But keep in mind, you can not use the estate money to do it. But the executor can use the estate money to defend himself. And if there was no will, the relatives have first crack at anything involved and they have every right to give the deceased girlfriend or boyfriend what ever they fell is fair or unfair. Unless she or he has receipts for what they bought that is in the house. Death and divorce bring out the worst in people.



</answer>

Do unions fight for their memebers rights and benefits?



Yes, and no.



Yes, they can shut down a plant by organizing a strike . . . . cool.



So they demand higher and higher, and HIGHER wages for their members, . . . . cool.



Now, in Detroit, Michigan, . . . auto workers putting one part on a car make a GREAT living . . . . cool



Only . . . their inflate salaries (for a job a well trained monkey could do) has driven the cost of a car so high that . . . . the plant is now going to close.



So . . . all of them are . . . . out of a job.



I believe unions did serve a purpose back in the day when there were no labor laws or agencies to protect worker SAFETY . . . but driving the salary higher and higher can effectively make the product being made too high, so, demad falls . . . . and all the people on the assembly line are OUT OF JOBS.



If you think I'm kidding . . . check out what is happening with auto workers in America.  (I take it you're in England based on how you spell "labour.")



Dan in Miami

<answer>



I don't live in CA, so my personal opinion won't be expressed in the election, but I still support it. Current CA law already gives homosexual couples in a civil union the EXACT same financial, legal, and social rights and obligations as a heterosexual married couple. Rights have nothing to do with the item on the ballot. Gays already have those rights.



It's about a label-- "married"-- that homosexuals have decided is the cool thing to have.



"Marriage is a gift that society bestows on its next generation. Marriage (and only marriage) unites the three core dimensions of parenthood -- biological, social and legal -- into one pro-child form: the married couple. Marriage says to a child: The man and the woman whose sexual union made you will also be there to love and raise you. Marriage says to society as a whole: For every child born, there is a recognized mother and a father, accountable to the child and to each other."



This is a quote from a great article that was in the LA Times about why Prop8 is a good idea, regardless of civil rights. It's in the 5th paragraph.



http://www.latimes.com/news/opinion/la-o...



If you don't want to click on the link, that's fine. I've copy/pasted the entire article.



*****
Protecting marriage to protect children
By David Blankenhorn



September 19, 2008



I'm a liberal Democrat. And I do not favor same-sex marriage. Do those positions sound contradictory? To me, they fit together.



Many seem to believe that marriage is simply a private love relationship between two people. They accept this view, in part, because Americans have increasingly emphasized and come to value the intimate, emotional side of marriage, and in part because almost all opinion leaders today, from journalists to judges, strongly embrace this position. That's certainly the idea that underpinned the California Supreme Court's legalization of same-sex marriage.



But I spent a year studying the history and anthropology of marriage, and I've come to a different conclusion.



Marriage as a human institution is constantly evolving, and many of its features vary across groups and cultures. But there is one constant. In all societies, marriage shapes the rights and obligations of parenthood. Among us humans, the scholars report, marriage is not primarily a license to have sex. Nor is it primarily a license to receive benefits or social recognition. It is primarily a license to have children.



In this sense, marriage is a gift that society bestows on its next generation. Marriage (and only marriage) unites the three core dimensions of parenthood -- biological, social and legal -- into one pro-child form: the married couple. Marriage says to a child: The man and the woman whose sexual union made you will also be there to love and raise you. Marriage says to society as a whole: For every child born, there is a recognized mother and a father, accountable to the child and to each other.



These days, because of the gay marriage debate, one can be sent to bed without supper for saying such things. But until very recently, almost no one denied this core fact about marriage. Summing up the cross-cultural evidence, the anthropologist Helen Fisher in 1992 put it simply: "People wed primarily to reproduce." The philosopher and Nobel laureate Bertrand Russell, certainly no friend of conventional sexual morality, was only repeating the obvious a few decades earlier when he concluded that "it is through children alone that sexual relations become important to society, and worthy to be taken cognizance of by a legal institution."



Marriage is society's most pro-child institution. In 2002 -- just moments before it became highly unfashionable to say so -- a team of researchers from Child Trends, a nonpartisan research center, reported that "family structure clearly matters for children, and the family structure that helps children the most is a family headed by two biological parents in a low-conflict marriage."



All our scholarly instruments seem to agree: For healthy development, what a child needs more than anything else is the mother and father who together made the child, who love the child and love each other.



For these reasons, children have the right, insofar as society can make it possible, to know and to be cared for by the two parents who brought them into this world. The foundational human rights document in the world today regarding children, the 1989 U.N. Convention on the Rights of the Child, specifically guarantees children this right. The last time I checked, liberals like me were supposed to be in favor of internationally recognized human rights, particularly concerning children, who are typically society's most voiceless and vulnerable group. Or have I now said something I shouldn't?



Every child being raised by gay or lesbian couples will be denied his birthright to both parents who made him. Every single one. Moreover, losing that right will not be a consequence of something that at least most of us view as tragic, such as a marriage that didn't last, or an unexpected pregnancy where the father-to-be has no intention of sticking around. On the contrary, in the case of same-sex marriage and the children of those unions, it will be explained to everyone, including the children, that something wonderful has happened!



For me, what we are encouraged or permitte



</answer>

<answer>



I don't really see that anyone has answered your actual question yet, so...



All judges are reviewed by the Judicial Commission and the Bar Association for where they live, and must adhere to both agency's rules as well at state and federal laws. When a judge passes sentence it must be in accordance with Federal Sentencing Guidelines. Should a judge do any act of misconduct, it can be reported to both the Bar association and the Judicial Commission, which will the review the data and investigate and determine a reprimand, if any at all.



</answer>



<answer from>



Caicos Turkey
Member since: 23 September 2006
Total points: 110636 (Level 7)



Contributing In:



Languages



Law & Ethics



Other - Society & Culture



</answer from>



<answer>



You may be interested in this article, which examines the situation of the judiciary around the world http://law.usc.edu/academics/assets/docs...



Judges are carefully appointed by the Judicial Commission and once a judge is appointed, he can only be removed if they are guilty of bad behaviour ("dum bene se gesserit" - While he shall conduct himself, during good behaviour). The only real protection against arbitrary decisions is the right of appeal and the fact that in the highest appellate court there is a panel of judges (five in the House of Lords) sitting on each case, so that if one judge goes out of line the others can out-vote him.



Judges are required to follow the law and to sentence in a way which is in keeping with previous sentencing for similar offences. They are also required to take account of mitigating circumstances, having far more information available to them than is available to the public at large from the various reports placed before them. However, there is no doubt that some decisions are surprising.



</answer>

<question from>



kankan92...
Member since: 30 July 2008
Total points: 170 (Level 1)



</question from>



<question>



If you scared somebody literally to death?



If you scared somebody so bad and resulted in their death, do you think you would go to jail for it, even though it was meant in fun and games? And if so how long and what would the charges be?



</question>



<answer from>



me
Member since: 15 December 2006
Total points: 14627 (Level 6)



</answer from>



<answer>



<evaluation>Best Answer - Chosen by Asker</evaluation>



if i got a aneurysm & died after reading this question can my  family charge you ?



</answer>



<asker's comment>



No your family can't charge me. But like the sarcasm.



</asker's comment>



<answer from>



Charles G
Member since: 21 July 2008
Total points: 6624 (Level 5)



Contributing In:



Law & Ethics



United States



</answer from>



<answer>



Well, you you scared them by shooting a bullet into him or her, you might be eligible for jail.



But being scared is not a usual cause of death for an autopsy.



If they charged you, they would need to do an autopsy, and what would be the cause of death? Heart attack? Stroke? I don't see how anyone could prosecute because of that.



To be prosecuted for premeditated murder, you have to intend to do it, and the state has to prove that you intended it.



Lesser charges of murder or manslaughter have lesser penalties.



</answer>



<answer from>



thornyy_...
Member since: 27 September 2008
Total points: 242 (Level 1)

so i dont see if you are fussing about with each other and one got so scared that they had a heart attack and died...there coudnt be a chance of going to jail.
